Book of Agency. Ashraf al-Hidayah Volume 10: Mutlaq (absolute). And if he attributes it to his own dirhams, it is for himself, construing his state according to what is lawful for him legally or what he customarily does, because buying for oneself while attributing the contract to another's dirhams is deemed objectionable legally and customarily. And if he attributes it to absolute dirhams, then if he intends it for the principal, it is for the principal; and if he intends it for himself, it is for himself, because he may act for himself and for the principal in this agency. And if they contradict each other regarding the intention, the payment is judged by consensus, because it is an explicit indication of what we mentioned. And if they agree that no intention was present, Muhammad said: It is for the contracting party (the agent), because the original rule is that everyone acts for himself unless it is proven that he made it for another, and it has not been proven. According to Abu Yusuf, the payment is judged in this case, because what he did absolutely can bear both possibilities, so it remains suspended. Then from whichever of the two funds he paid, it becomes for that person, and because with their mutual agreement [on no intention], the possibility of intention for the principal remains. And in what we have said, his state is construed as being correct, just as in the case of mutual contradiction. And the agency for Islam (salam) is also subject to these aspects. Translation: And if the principal appointed him as an agent to buy an unspecified slave, and the agent bought a slave, then it will be for the agent, unless he says 'I intended to buy for the principal' or he buys it with the principal's property. The author of Hidayah said: This issue has several forms. If the agent attributes the dirhams to the principal, then the contract is for the principal. And in my view, this is what is meant by the author's (Quduri's) statement 'or he buys it with the property of the principal', not the payment from the principal's property, because there is detail and disagreement in that, and this ruling is by consensus, although it is absolute. And if the agent attributes the contract of purchase to his own dirhams, then the contract of purchase is for the agent, construing his state upon what is lawful for him legally or what he customarily does. Because buying for oneself with the contract attributed to another's dirhams is legally and customarily objectionable. And if the agent attributes the contract of purchase to absolute dirhams, then if he intends it for the principal, it is for the principal; and if he intends it for himself, it is for himself, because in this agency it is permissible for the agent to act for himself or for the principal. And if the agent and principal contradict each other in intention, the payment is judged by consensus, because it is a clear indication of what we mentioned. And if they agree that no intention was present, Imam Muhammad said: The contract of purchase is for the contracting party (agent), because the original rule is that everyone acts for himself, unless it is proven that he made his work for another, and this has not been proven. And according to Imam Abu Yusuf, in this case too the payment is judged, because what the agent did absolutely can bear both possibilities, so it remains suspended. Then from whose property the price is paid, it is as if he did the work for that person. And because with their agreement on no intention, the possibility of intention for the principal remains, just as in the case of mutual contradiction. And the agency for salam (forward sale) of grain is also subject to these forms. Explanation: The form of the issue is that if a person appointed another as agent to buy an unspecified slave, then the agent bought a slave, the purchase will be for the agent, and the agent will be the owner of that slave. But if the agent says 'I intended to buy for the principal', or the agent buys this slave with the property of the principal, then in both these cases the slave will be for the principal. The author of Hidayah says: This issue has three forms, because the agent may attribute the contract of purchase to the principal's dirhams, e.g. he says 'I bought this slave in exchange for the principal's dirhams'; or to his own dirhams, e.g. he says 'I bought this slave in exchange for my own dirhams'; or to absolute dirhams, e.g. he says 'I bought this slave in exchange for one hundred dirhams' without saying 'my dirhams' or 'the principal's dirhams', i.e. he did not attribute the dirhams to himself or the principal. The author of Hidayah says: In Quduri's expression 'or he buys it with the property of the principal', there are two possibilities: one is that the agent pays the price from the principal's property without attributing the contract to him; the second is that the agent attributes the contract of purchase to the principal's dirhams. The commentator says: In my view, the second possibility is intended, because in the first possibility there is detail and disagreement, as will come later. And the second possibility, that if the agent attributes the contract to the principal's dirhams, then the contract will occur for the principal, is a matter of consensus and agreement. And Quduri's expression 'or he buys it with the property of the principal' is absolute, i.e. no disagreement is mentioned in it. So when no disagreement is mentioned, it will be construed upon the agreed and consensus form, i.e. it will be said that by Quduri's expression 'or he buys it with the property of the principal' is meant attributing the contract of purchase to the principal's dirhams, not paying the price from the principal's property without attributing the contract. In any case, among the three mentioned forms: the first form is that if the agent attributes the contract of purchase to the principal's dirhams, then the contract occurs for the principal, and the purchased slave belongs to the principal, not the agent. The second form is that if the agent attributes the contract of purchase to his own dirhams, then the contract is for the agent himself, and the purchased slave belongs to the agent, not the principal. The author of Hidayah, after these two issues, mentioned two proofs from 'construing his state upon what is lawful for him legally or what he customarily does'. One proof is custom and the other is legal. The custom proof includes both, because it is current that one who attributes the contract to his own dirhams is one who buys for himself, and one who attributes it to the principal's dirhams is one who buys for the principal. So due to this custom, in the first form the contract of purchase will be for the principal, and in the second form for the agent. And the legal proof is specifically related to the first form, in that the first form is that if the agent attributes the contract of purchase to the principal's dirhams, then the contract of purchase will be for the principal, because if the contract of purchase is attributed to another's property and the purchase is for oneself, this is forbidden, because in that case...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on a ruling from Quduri's Mukhtasar regarding the appointment of an agent to buy an unspecified slave. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute (between Muhammad al-Shaybani and Abu Yusuf) embedded within a broader exposition of the ruling's scenarios. The commentator, following the Hidayah, first lays out three distinct scenarios based on how the agent attributes the dirhams in the purchase contract:
1. Attribution to the principal's dirhams: the purchase is for the principal, by consensus, because it is both legally problematic and customarily improper for someone to buy for himself while attributing the contract to another's property.
2. Attribution to the agent's own dirhams: the purchase is for the agent, by the same reasoning.
3. Attribution to absolute dirhams (without specifying): the ruling depends on intention. If the agent intends for the principal, it is for the principal; if for himself, it is for himself. If they dispute intention, the court looks at whose money actually paid the price (by consensus). If they agree that no intention was present, there is disagreement: Muhammad says the purchase is for the agent (since the default is that one acts for oneself), while Abu Yusuf says judgment is by payment (since the absolute action is suspended and the payment actualizes it). The commentator then discusses the interpretation of Quduri's phrase 'or he buys it with the property of the principal', arguing that it refers to attributing the contract to the principal's dirhams, not merely paying from his property, because the latter has further detail and disagreement. Finally, two proofs are given: custom ('urf) and legal reasoning (shar'): custom indicates that attribution of dirhams signals the intended beneficiary; legal reason forbids buying for oneself with another's property in the contract's wording. The entire discussion remains within the Hanafi school, with no inter-madhhab comparison.

Key Terms
وکیل (wakil): The agent appointed to perform a task on behalf of the principal.
موکل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints the agent.
عقد شراء (aqd al-shira'): The contract of purchase made by the agent.
دراہم مطلقہ (diraham mutlaqa): Absolute dirhams; dirhams mentioned without attribution to a specific party.
نیت (niyyah): Intention of the agent regarding for whom he is purchasing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An agent appointed to buy a slave says 'I bought this slave with my own dirhams.' According to the Hanafi school, who owns the slave?
A) The principal, because the agent was appointed for that purpose.
B) The agent, because the contract was attributed to his own dirhams.
C) It depends on who actually paid the price.
D) The slave is shared equally.

Key Takeaway
Attribution of dirhams in the contract determines ownership: principal's dirhams → for principal, agent's dirhams → for agent, by consensus and dual proofs.

10 min
Absolute Dirhams with No Intention: The Muhammad vs Abu Yusuf Dispute
Exposes the intra-Hanafi dispute on absolute dirhams with no intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the ikhtilaf between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf when an agent attributes to absolute dirhams and both agree no intention was present, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
When no intention and absolute dirhams, Muhammad says agent owns; Abu Yusuf says payment decides.

This lesson builds on the three attribution scenarios. When the agent attributes to absolute dirhams (without mentioning principal or self), intention normally decides. But if both parties agree that no intention was present, an intra-Hanafi dispute arises between Imam Muhammad and Abu Yusuf. Understanding this dispute sharpens one's grasp of default rules and evidentiary hierarchy.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the initial rule
The agent attributes the purchase to absolute dirhams (no mention of principal or self). If he has an intention, it follows that intention. But if both agree no intention was present, the ikhtilaf begins.
2
Imam Muhammad's position and proof
Muhammad says the purchase belongs to the agent (the contracting party). The proof: the original rule (asl) is that everyone acts for himself; it is not proven that he acted for another.
This is the default principle.
3
Abu Yusuf's position and proof
Abu Yusuf says the contract is suspended and payment determines ownership. The proof: the absolute action can bear both possibilities, so it remains suspended until payment actualizes it for one party.
Also, the mutual agreement on no intention still allows possibility of intention for the principal.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
If the agent pays from the principal's money, Muhammad says the slave is for the agent (default), while Abu Yusuf says it is for the principal (payment determines). This is the key difference.
5
Identify the tarjih (preferred view)
The Hidayah presents Muhammad's view first, and it is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school.
Later works and the commentator support this tarjih.

Book of Agency. Ashraf al-Hidayah Volume 10: Mutlaq (absolute). And if he attributes it to his own dirhams, it is for himself, construing his state according to what is lawful for him legally or what he customarily does, because buying for oneself while attributing the contract to another's dirhams is deemed objectionable legally and customarily. And if he attributes it to absolute dirhams, then if he intends it for the principal, it is for the principal; and if he intends it for himself, it is for himself, because he may act for himself and for the principal in this agency. And if they contradict each other regarding the intention, the payment is judged by consensus, because it is an explicit indication of what we mentioned. And if they agree that no intention was present, Muhammad said: It is for the contracting party (the agent), because the original rule is that everyone acts for himself unless it is proven that he made it for another, and it has not been proven. According to Abu Yusuf, the payment is judged in this case, because what he did absolutely can bear both possibilities, so it remains suspended. Then from whichever of the two funds he paid, it becomes for that person, and because with their mutual agreement [on no intention], the possibility of intention for the principal remains. And in what we have said, his state is construed as being correct, just as in the case of mutual contradiction. And the agency for Islam (salam) is also subject to these aspects. Translation: And if the principal appointed him as an agent to buy an unspecified slave, and the agent bought a slave, then it will be for the agent, unless he says 'I intended to buy for the principal' or he buys it with the principal's property. The author of Hidayah said: This issue has several forms. If the agent attributes the dirhams to the principal, then the contract is for the principal. And in my view, this is what is meant by the author's (Quduri's) statement 'or he buys it with the property of the principal', not the payment from the principal's property, because there is detail and disagreement in that, and this ruling is by consensus, although it is absolute. And if the agent attributes the contract of purchase to his own dirhams, then the contract of purchase is for the agent, construing his state upon what is lawful for him legally or what he customarily does. Because buying for oneself with the contract attributed to another's dirhams is legally and customarily objectionable. And if the agent attributes the contract of purchase to absolute dirhams, then if he intends it for the principal, it is for the principal; and if he intends it for himself, it is for himself, because in this agency it is permissible for the agent to act for himself or for the principal. And if the agent and principal contradict each other in intention, the payment is judged by consensus, because it is a clear indication of what we mentioned. And if they agree that no intention was present, Imam Muhammad said: The contract of purchase is for the contracting party (agent), because the original rule is that everyone acts for himself, unless it is proven that he made his work for another, and this has not been proven. And according to Imam Abu Yusuf, in this case too the payment is judged, because what the agent did absolutely can bear both possibilities, so it remains suspended. Then from whose property the price is paid, it is as if he did the work for that person. And because with their agreement on no intention, the possibility of intention for the principal remains, just as in the case of mutual contradiction. And the agency for salam (forward sale) of grain is also subject to these forms. Explanation: The form of the issue is that if a person appointed another as agent to buy an unspecified slave, then the agent bought a slave, the purchase will be for the agent, and the agent will be the owner of that slave. But if the agent says 'I intended to buy for the principal', or the agent buys this slave with the property of the principal, then in both these cases the slave will be for the principal. The author of Hidayah says: This issue has three forms, because the agent may attribute the contract of purchase to the principal's dirhams, e.g. he says 'I bought this slave in exchange for the principal's dirhams'; or to his own dirhams, e.g. he says 'I bought this slave in exchange for my own dirhams'; or to absolute dirhams, e.g. he says 'I bought this slave in exchange for one hundred dirhams' without saying 'my dirhams' or 'the principal's dirhams', i.e. he did not attribute the dirhams to himself or the principal. The author of Hidayah says: In Quduri's expression 'or he buys it with the property of the principal', there are two possibilities: one is that the agent pays the price from the principal's property without attributing the contract to him; the second is that the agent attributes the contract of purchase to the principal's dirhams. The commentator says: In my view, the second possibility is intended, because in the first possibility there is detail and disagreement, as will come later. And the second possibility, that if the agent attributes the contract to the principal's dirhams, then the contract will occur for the principal, is a matter of consensus and agreement. And Quduri's expression 'or he buys it with the property of the principal' is absolute, i.e. no disagreement is mentioned in it. So when no disagreement is mentioned, it will be construed upon the agreed and consensus form, i.e. it will be said that by Quduri's expression 'or he buys it with the property of the principal' is meant attributing the contract of purchase to the principal's dirhams, not paying the price from the principal's property without attributing the contract. In any case, among the three mentioned forms: the first form is that if the agent attributes the contract of purchase to the principal's dirhams, then the contract occurs for the principal, and the purchased slave belongs to the principal, not the agent. The second form is that if the agent attributes the contract of purchase to his own dirhams, then the contract is for the agent himself, and the purchased slave belongs to the agent, not the principal. The author of Hidayah, after these two issues, mentioned two proofs from 'construing his state upon what is lawful for him legally or what he customarily does'. One proof is custom and the other is legal. The custom proof includes both, because it is current that one who attributes the contract to his own dirhams is one who buys for himself, and one who attributes it to the principal's dirhams is one who buys for the principal. So due to this custom, in the first form the contract of purchase will be for the principal, and in the second form for the agent. And the legal proof is specifically related to the first form, in that the first form is that if the agent attributes the contract of purchase to the principal's dirhams, then the contract of purchase will be for the principal, because if the contract of purchase is attributed to another's property and the purchase is for oneself, this is forbidden, because in that case...
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary on a ruling from Quduri's Mukhtasar regarding the appointment of an agent to buy an unspecified slave. The passage type is an intra-Hanafi dispute (between Muhammad al-Shaybani and Abu Yusuf) embedded within a broader exposition of the ruling's scenarios. The commentator, following the Hidayah, first lays out three distinct scenarios based on how the agent attributes the dirhams in the purchase contract:
1. Attribution to the principal's dirhams: the purchase is for the principal, by consensus, because it is both legally problematic and customarily improper for someone to buy for himself while attributing the contract to another's property.
2. Attribution to the agent's own dirhams: the purchase is for the agent, by the same reasoning.
3. Attribution to absolute dirhams (without specifying): the ruling depends on intention. If the agent intends for the principal, it is for the principal; if for himself, it is for himself. If they dispute intention, the court looks at whose money actually paid the price (by consensus). If they agree that no intention was present, there is disagreement: Muhammad says the purchase is for the agent (since the default is that one acts for oneself), while Abu Yusuf says judgment is by payment (since the absolute action is suspended and the payment actualizes it). The commentator then discusses the interpretation of Quduri's phrase 'or he buys it with the property of the principal', arguing that it refers to attributing the contract to the principal's dirhams, not merely paying from his property, because the latter has further detail and disagreement. Finally, two proofs are given: custom ('urf) and legal reasoning (shar'): custom indicates that attribution of dirhams signals the intended beneficiary; legal reason forbids buying for oneself with another's property in the contract's wording. The entire discussion remains within the Hanafi school, with no inter-madhhab comparison.

Key Terms
اصل (asl): The default rule or principle; here, that a person acts for himself unless proven otherwise.
موقوف (mawquf): Suspended; a contract or action that remains pending until a condition (like payment) is fulfilled.
ثمرة الخلاف (thamarah al-khilaf): The practical consequence of the scholarly disagreement; here, the difference in ownership when payment is from the principal's money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An agent buys a slave saying 'I bought this for 100 dirhams' without specifying. Both agent and principal later agree they had no intention. The agent pays from the principal's money. What is the mufta bihi Hanafi ruling?
A) The slave belongs to the agent, because the default is that one acts for himself.
B) The slave belongs to the principal, because payment determines ownership.
C) The slave belongs to neither until payment is resolved.
D) The slave is invalidly purchased.

Key Takeaway
When an agent attributes to absolute dirhams and no intention exists, the mufta bihi is that the purchase belongs to the agent, per Imam Muhammad's default principle.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Agent's Credibility When Price Unpaid
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agent's credibility when price unpaid

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios of agent-principal dispute regarding an unspecified slave purchase and explain the mufta bihi position according to Imam Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
An agent's credibility hinges on his status as amin or mutahham, determined by whether the price has been paid.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute about whose word is taken when an agent appointed to buy an unspecified slave later claims he bought it for the principal, but the principal denies it. The ruling pivots on the payment of the price, which determines the agent's legal status as an amin (trustee) or mutahham (suspect). Understanding this dispute clarifies the Hanafi procedural rules regarding claims of trustees and the burden of proof in agency conflicts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute scenario
An agent is appointed to buy an unspecified slave. After purchase, the agent says he bought it for the principal; the principal says the agent bought it for himself. The slave is alive. Determine whose statement is accepted.
The scenario hinges on whether the price (thaman) has already been paid by the principal to the agent.
2
Distinguish the three sub-scenarios
Based on payment status: (1) Price paid → agent is amin, his statement is accepted by all. (2) Price unpaid → agent is not amin, dispute between Sahibayn and Abu Hanifa. (3) Agent's ability to repurchase → Sahibayn argue this removes suspicion.
The passage covers all scenarios; focus on the unpaid price dispute.
3
Apply the principle of amin vs mutahham
When price is paid, the agent holds the principal's money, making him a trustee (amin) seeking discharge. His statement is credible per rules of trust. When price is unpaid, he is not an amin; his statement is open to suspicion (tuhma).
The key is whether the agent has possession of the price; if not, he is mutahham.
4
Compare the arguments of Sahibayn and Abu Hanifa
Sahibayn argue the agent can repurchase the slave for the principal by rescinding the first contract, so he is not suspect. Abu Hanifa argues the original possibility of self-purchase creates suspicion that cannot be removed by a future repurchase.
The objection and response about rescission is part of the debate.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
If agent's statement is accepted (Sahibayn), principal must pay and take the slave. If principal's statement is accepted (Abu Hanifa), agent must keep the slave or compensate. Later Hanafi works often prefer Abu Hanifa's view.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is who bears the financial loss if the slave is defective or has lost value.

اعتراض لیکن اگر اس پر اعتراض کرے کہ اگر خریداری اولا وکیل کیلئے ہو جیسا کہ موکل کہتا ہے تو اس کے بعد از سرنو موکل کیلئے خریداری کیسے ہو سکتی ہے۔ جواب ... اس کا جواب یہ ہے کہ وکیل اپنے بائع کے ساتھ عقد شراء کو مسخ کر دے پھر اس کو مؤکل کے لئے خرید لے اس طرح کوئی اعتراض واقع نہ ہوگا۔ حضرت امام اعظم کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ وکیل کا یہ خبر دینا کہ میں نے یہ غلام موکل کیلئے خریدا ہے محل تہمت ہے اس طور پر کہ وکیل نے اس غلام کو اصلا تو اپنے لئے ہی خریدا ہو مگر جب اس میں خسارہ محسوس ہوا ہو تو اس کو مؤکل کے ذمہ ڈالنے کا ارادہ کر لیا ہو۔ پس اس تہمت کی وجہ سے وکیل کا قول معتبر نہ ہو گا۔ بلکہ مؤکل کا قول معتبر ہو گا۔ اسکے برخلاف جب مؤکل ، وکیل کو ثمن ادا کر چکا اور پھر وکیل اور موکل میں اختلاف ہوا تو وکیل کا قول اسلئے معتبر ہو گا کہ اس صورت میں وکیل امین ہے اور عہدہ امانت سے سبکدوش ہونے میں امین ہی کا قول معتبر ہوتا ہے۔ لہذا یہاں بھی وکیل جو امین ہے امانت سے عہدہ برآ ہونے میں اس کا قول معتبر ہوگا اور اس کے ضمن اور تبعیت میں وکیل کا یہ قول بھی معتبر اور قبول ہوگا کہ غلام کی خریداری مؤکل کیلئے ہے اور جو چیز ضمناً تبعاً ثابت ہوتی ہے اس کے وصف کی طرف نظر نہیں کی جاتی ۔ لہذا اس صورت میں وکیل کے متہم یا غیر متہم ہونے کی طرف نظر نہ کی جائے گی۔ بلکہ وکیل کا قول قبول کر لیا جائے گا۔ اور یہاں یعنی جس صورت میں موکل نے وکیل کو ثمن نہیں دیا ہے اور غلام زندہ ہے اس صورت میں وکیل کے قبضہ میں چونکہ ثمن نہیں ہے اسلئے وکیل امین بھی نہ ہوگا۔ اور جب اس صورت میں وکیل امین نہیں ہے تو اس کا قول بھی معتبر نہ ہوگا۔
Answer: The answer is that by renewing the purchase contract, the agent's aim is to take the price from the principal, not to make a purchase contract for the principal. The author of al-Hidayah clarified the intended meaning by leaving the intermediary (the contract) and mentioning the objective. So this falls under the category of mentioning the cause (recourse against the principal for the price) while intending the (actual) cause (purchase contract). And this is permissible. In the second scenario, i.e., when the principal has already paid the price to the agent, in that case the agent is a trustee (amin), and by saying "I had bought a slave for you but he died with me," he wants to discharge himself from the responsibility of the trust. In matters of trust, if a dispute occurs between the owner and the trustee—the trustee claiming to be discharged from the trust and the owner denying it—the trustee's statement is credible. So here too, since the principal, who is the owner of the money, claims that you did not buy the slave for me but bought it for yourself, therefore you have not discharged the trust, while the agent, who is the trustee, claims that I bought this slave for you, therefore I have discharged the trust responsibility. So here too the trustee's, i.e., the agent's, statement will be credible. The author of al-Hidayah said: When the principal and agent dispute—the agent saying 'I bought this slave for you' and the principal saying 'No, you bought it for yourself'—if the slave is alive and the principal has given the price to the agent, then the agent's statement is credible because the agent is a trustee seeking to discharge himself from the trust, and it has passed earlier that the trustee's statement is credible. Therefore, in this case too the agent's statement will be credible. And if the principal has not given the price to the agent, then according to the two companions (Sahibayn), in this case too the agent's statement is credible. But according to Imam al-A'zam (Abu Hanifa), the principal's statement is credible. The proof of the two companions is that in this case the agent is able to make a new purchase contract for the principal because the slave is alive and a living slave is capable of being purchased. Therefore, the agent is currently able to buy this slave for the principal, and when the agent is able to buy this slave for the principal at present, then the agent is not suspect (mutahham) in his report that he bought this slave for the principal. When the agent is not suspect in his report, then his statement is definitely credible. Objection: But if someone objects that if the purchase was initially for the agent, as the principal says, then how can it be subsequently purchased anew for the principal? Answer: The answer is that the agent can rescind the purchase contract with the seller and then buy it for the principal; thus no objection arises. The proof of Imam al-A'zam (Abu Hanifa) is that the agent's giving this news—that he bought this slave for the principal—is a place of suspicion, in the sense that the agent initially bought the slave for himself, but when he felt a loss in it, he intended to pass it on to the principal. Therefore, due to this suspicion, the agent's statement is not credible; rather, the principal's statement is credible. In contrast, when the principal has paid the price to the agent and then the agent and principal dispute, the agent's statement is credible because in that case the agent is a trustee, and in discharging himself from the trust, the trustee's statement is credible. Hence, here too the agent, who is a trustee, his statement regarding discharge from the trust is credible, and implicitly and consequently, this statement of the agent—that the purchase was for the principal—will also be credible and accepted. And when a thing is established implicitly and consequently, attention is not paid to its quality. Therefore, in this case, attention will not be paid to whether the agent is suspect or not; rather, the agent's statement will be accepted. And here—i.e., in the case where the principal has not given the price to the agent and the slave is alive—since the agent does not have the price in his possession, he is not a trustee. And when in this case the agent is not a trustee, his statement is also not credible.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the credibility of the agent's claim when he purchases an unspecified slave and a dispute arises between him and the principal. The discussion explores the legal reasoning behind whose statement is accepted, focusing on the status of the agent as either a trustee (amin) or a suspect (mutahham). The text presents three scenarios: (1) when the price has been paid, all agree the agent's statement is credible because he is a trustee seeking discharge; (2) when the price has not been paid, the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) maintain the agent's statement is credible because he can repurchase the slave for the principal, removing suspicion; (3) Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the principal's statement is credible because the agent is suspect—he may have bought the slave for himself and later tries to pass a loss onto the principal. The objection that the agent cannot repurchase if he initially bought for himself is answered by noting that the agent can rescind the first contract and then purchase anew. The passage clarifies that the ruling hinges on the agent's status as an amin: when the price is paid, he is an amin and his word is taken; when unpaid, he is not an amin, and there is a dispute. The relied-upon position in the Hanafi school for fatwa, as per later texts, is generally that of Imam Abu Hanifa, although the passage does not state a preference explicitly.

Key Terms
وکیل (wakīl): Agent appointed by the principal to perform a transaction (e.g., buying a slave). The text discusses when his statement about the transaction is credible.
موکل (muwakkil): Principal who appoints the agent; the person for whom the purchase is allegedly made. The text considers his claim against the agent.
ثمن (thaman): Price paid by the principal to the agent for the purchase. The payment status determines whether the agent is an amin.
امین (amīn): Trustee; a person entrusted with property who is not liable for loss except by negligence. The agent becomes amin upon receiving the price. His statement in discharge is credible.
متہم (mutahham): Suspect; a person whose claim is doubted because of a potential conflict of interest. In this case, the agent is suspect when he might have bought for himself and then changes intention.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, in a dispute between agent and principal over an unspecified slave purchase, if the price has NOT been paid and the slave is alive, whose statement is accepted?
A) The agent's statement, because he is an amin
B) The principal's statement, because the agent is suspect
C) The agent's statement, because he can repurchase the slave
D) The principal's statement, because the agent is an amin

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an agent's credibility in disputes over the purpose of a purchase depends on whether he holds the principal's price—if paid, he is an amin and his word is taken; if unpaid, he is suspect, and the principal's word prevails (Imam Abu Hanifa's view).

Agent's Statement Accepted by Consensus for Specified Slave
Ruling with consensus for specified slave: agent's claim credible

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the agent's credibility when disputing ownership of a specified slave, and explain why the absence of suspicion (tuhma) leads to acceptance of the agent's claim.

Core Idea
When the slave is specified, the agent cannot buy it for himself—so his claim is free from suspicion and accepted by consensus.

In this passage, we examine a unique scenario where the Hanafi school is unanimous: if a principal appoints an agent to buy a specific slave, and a dispute later arises about who the purchase was for, the agent's word is taken over the principal's. This ruling applies whether the price was paid or not. The key reason is that the agent, being restricted to that exact slave, has no personal incentive to lie, because he could not lawfully buy that same slave for himself at that price in the principal's absence. Thus, there is no suspicion (tuhma) to undermine his credibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is the agent's statement claiming he bought the specified slave for the principal. The ruling is that his statement is accepted as credible and binding on the principal.
The ruling is procedural, not a taklifi hukm.
2
Note the conditions
The slave must be specified (mu'ayyan) and alive at the time of the dispute. The dispute concerns whether the purchase was for the principal or the agent himself.
If the slave is unspecified, the ruling differs.
3
Understand the proof
The proof is consensus (ijma) of the three Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The text states 'and this is by consensus'.
In Hanafi usul, ijma of the fuqaha is a definitive (qat'i) proof.
4
Analyze the rationale
The agent reported something he can do anew (buy the slave again) and there is no suspicion because he cannot buy that specified slave for himself at the same price in the principal's absence.
The absence of tuhma is the key factor.
5
Contrast with unspecified slave
If the slave is unspecified, the agent could have bought a similar slave for himself, creating suspicion. Thus, there is ikhtilaf among the imams for that case.
This contrast highlights the importance of specificity.

کو ثمن دیا ہو یا نہ دیا ہو۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ وکیل نے یہ کہ کر کہ میں نے یہ غلام مؤکل کیلئے خریدا ہے ایسی چیز کی خبر دی ہے جو اس کو وہ از سر نو کر سکتا ہے یعنی اختلاف کے بعد وکیل اس غلام کو مؤکل کیلئے از سر نوخرید کر سکتا ہے اور وکیل اس خبر دینے میں متہم بھی نہ ہوگا۔ اور وکیل اس لئے متہم نہ ہوگا کہ جو شخص کسی متعینہ چیز کو خریدنے کا وکیل ہو وہ موکل کی عدم موجودگی میں اس ثمن کے بقدر کے عوض اس شے کو اپنے لئے خریدنے کا مجاز نہیں ہوتا۔ جیسا کہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے۔ پس جب وکیل اس متعینہ غلام کو اپنے لئے خریدنے کا مجاز نہیں ہے تو وہ اپنی اس خبر میں کہ میں نے یہ غلام مؤکل کیلئے خریدا ہے قطعا متہم نہ ہوگا۔ اس کے برخلاف اگر غیر معین غلام خریدنے کا وکیل کیا ہو اور پھر وکیل یہ کہے کہ میں نے یہ غلام مؤکل کیلئے خریدا ہے تو وکیل اپنی اس خبر میں متہم ہو سکتا ہے جیسا کہ گذشتہ مسئلہ کے تحت امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل میں تفصیل سے بیان کیا گیا ہے۔
And if he ordered him to buy a specified slave, then they disputed, and the slave is alive, then the statement is for the one ordered (the agent), whether the price is paid or not. And this is by consensus, because he reported something that he is able to do anew, and there is no suspicion in it, because the agent for buying a specified thing does not have the right to buy it for himself at that same price in the absence of the principal, as has passed, contrary to the unspecified slave, as we mentioned for Abu Hanifa. Translation: And if the principal ordered the agent to buy a specific slave, then both disputed while the slave is alive, then the agent's statement will be accepted, whether the price is paid or not. And this ruling is by consensus, because the agent reported something that he is able to do anew, and there is no suspicion in it, because the agent for buying a specific thing is not authorized to buy it for himself at the equivalent of that price in the absence of the principal, as has passed. Contrary to the unspecified slave, as we explained in the proof of Abu Hanifa. Explanation: One scenario of the previous issue is that the principal ordered the agent to buy a specific slave. Then both disputed—the principal said, 'You bought this slave for yourself,' and the agent said, 'No, I bought it for you'—and at the time of dispute the slave is alive. Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students, Imam Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad, agree that in this case the agent's statement is credible, whether the principal gave the price or not. The proof is that by saying 'I bought this slave for the principal,' the agent reported something that he can do anew—that is, after the dispute, the agent can buy this slave anew for the principal—and the agent is not suspect in giving this news. And the agent is not suspect because a person who is agent for buying a specific thing is not authorized to buy it for himself at the equivalent of that price in the absence of the principal, as has passed. So when the agent is not authorized to buy this specific slave for himself, then he is absolutely not suspect in his news that he bought this slave for the principal. In contrast, if he was appointed agent for an unspecified slave and then the agent says 'I bought this slave for the principal,' then the agent can be suspect in his news, as was explained in detail in the proof of Imam Abu Hanifa in the previous issue.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage provides a ruling with consensus (ijma) for a specific scenario: when the principal appoints an agent to buy a particular (mu'ayyan) slave, and after purchase a dispute arises about whether the agent bought for himself or for the principal, with the slave still alive. In this case, the agent's statement that he bought for the principal is accepted by all Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani), regardless of whether the price has been paid or not. The reason is that the agent, being limited to buying a specific item, cannot lawfully buy that same item for himself at the same price while the principal is absent; thus, he has no personal interest in the transaction, and his claim is free from suspicion (tuhma). This contrasts with the case of an unspecified slave, where the agent could have bought a similar slave for himself, leading to suspicion. The passage also notes that the agent could repurchase the slave if needed, further removing any suspicion. The ruling is presented as an exception to the earlier dispute, underscoring the importance of the specificity of the object in the agency contract.

Key Terms
معین (mu'ayyan): Specified; a particular slave that is identified. The agent is appointed to buy that specific slave, not any slave of a certain description.
بالاجماع (bil-ijmā'): By consensus; the ruling that the agent's statement is accepted is agreed upon by Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
تہمت (tuhma): Suspicion; a reason to doubt the agent's claim. In this case, the agent is not suspected because he cannot buy the specified slave for himself at that price.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the reason the agent's statement is accepted by consensus when buying a specified slave?
A) The agent is always an amin
B) The principal has paid the price
C) The agent cannot buy the specified slave for himself at that price in the principal's absence
D) The slave is dead

Key Takeaway
For a specified purchase, the agent's statement is accepted as free from suspicion, based on consensus among the Hanafi imams.

Agent's Statement Accepted in Purchase of Specific Slave
Ruling on dispute over purchase of specific slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a principal and agent dispute over the purchase of a specific slave and explain the rational proof that supports the agent's statement being accepted.

Core Idea
For a specific ordered item, the agent's word is accepted — he cannot legally buy it for himself at the same price.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a common dispute in agency: when the principal claims the agent bought a specific slave for himself, but the agent claims for the principal. The text rules definitively that the agent's statement is accepted, and this is by consensus among the Hanafi imams. The reasoning is a clear rational proof based on the agent's lack of motive and ability to redo the purchase.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed scenario
The principal appoints an agent to buy a specific slave (mu'ayyan). After purchase, they dispute: principal claims agent bought for himself, agent claims for principal. The slave is alive.
The specificity of the slave (identified by name or description) is crucial.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that the agent's statement (qawl al-ma'mur) is accepted, whether the price was paid or not.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position.
3
Note the consensus
This ruling is by consensus (bil-ijma') among the Hanafi imams — Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani all agree.
For non-specific items, there is ikhtilaf, but for specific, it is unanimous.
4
Analyze the rational proof (qiyas)
Two reasons: (1) The agent can initiate anew — he can legally repurchase the slave for the principal, so his claim is about something feasible. (2) There is no suspicion (tuhmah) — the agent cannot buy that specific slave for himself at the same price in the principal's absence, so he has no motive to lie.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on the nature of agency.
5
Contrast with non-specific items
For a non-specific slave (ghayr mu'ayyan), Abu Hanifa holds that the agent's statement is not accepted due to suspicion of self-dealing. The Sahibayn accept it. But for specific, all agree.
This contrast highlights the importance of specificity in removing suspicion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان کان امره بشراء عبد بعينه ثم اختلفا والعبد حى فالقول للمامور سواء كان الثمن منقوداً أو غير منقود وهذا بالاجماع لانه اخبر عما يملك استينافه ولا تهمة فيه لان الوكيل بشراء شيء بعينه لا يملك شراءه لنفسه بمثل ذالك الثمن في حال غيبته على ما مر بخلاف غير المعين على ما ذكرناه لابي حنيفة
And if he ordered him to buy a specific slave, then they disputed and the slave is alive, then the statement is of the one commanded, whether the price was paid or not paid, and this is by consensus, because he informed about something he can initiate anew, and there is no suspicion in it, because the agent for buying a specific thing cannot buy it for himself for the same price in his absence, as previously mentioned, contrary to the non-specific, as we mentioned for Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage states a ruling: When a principal appoints an agent to buy a specific slave and a dispute arises (the principal claims the agent bought it for himself, the agent claims for the principal) while the slave is alive, the agent's statement is accepted, whether the price was handed over or not. This is by consensus (ijma') among the Hanafi imams. The reasoning: the agent's claim is about something he can still do anew (he can repurchase the same slave for the principal), and there is no suspicion of self-dealing because an agent appointed to buy a specific item cannot legally buy that exact item for himself at the same price in the principal's absence. This contrasts with buying a non-specific slave, where there is a dispute (Abu Hanifa holds the agent's statement is not accepted due to suspicion; the Sahibayn accept it). Thus, for specific items, the rule is settled.

Key Terms
غلام معين (ghulam mu'ayyan): A specific, identified slave (e.g., by name or description).
تہمت (tuhmah): Suspicion or accusation of self-dealing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The principal's statement is accepted if he paid the price.
B) The agent's statement is accepted by consensus, regardless of price payment.
C) The agent's statement is accepted only if he can prove he acted as agent.
D) Neither statement is accepted; the slave is returned to the seller.

Key Takeaway
When an agent is appointed to buy a specific item, his statement in a dispute is accepted because he can renew the purchase and has no motive to lie.

Absolute Agency: Buying One of Two Specified Slaves
States the ruling that buying one of two specified slaves under absolute agency is valid by consensus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on an agent buying one of two specified slaves under absolute agency and identify the basis in ijma.

Core Idea
An absolute agency permits buying one of two specified items; no breach if price is fair.

This lesson examines a concise ruling from the Hanafi matn. The scenario: a principal authorizes an agent to buy two specific slaves without naming a price. The agent buys only one. The text declares this valid by consensus. We will unpack the reasoning based on the absoluteness of the agency and the condition of fair dealing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Ruling
Read the passage and identify the principal's instruction and the agent's action. State the ruling: the purchase of one slave is valid (jai'iz).
The ruling is unconditional except for the fairness condition.
2
Locate the Dalil (Proof)
The text cites ijma (consensus) as the proof. Find the phrase 'وهذا كله بالاجماع' (and all this is by consensus).
Ijma is a primary source of law for the Hanafi school.
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
Understand how consensus proves the ruling. The absence of recorded dissent among Hanafi imams establishes the ruling as binding.
The ruling is qat'i (certain) in transmission and meaning.
4
Understand the Reasoning (Ta'lil)
The agency is absolute (mutlaq), so it operates according to its generality. The agent may buy one or both. The only restriction is that the price must be fair (no taghabun).
Absoluteness allows discretion unless explicitly restricted.
5
Apply to Similar Cases
Think of modern scenarios: an agent authorized to buy one of two phones or two houses. Apply the same ruling: purchase of one is valid.
The principle applies to any specified item, not just slaves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال و من امر رجلا بان يشترى له عبدين باعيانهما ولم يسم له ثمنا فاشترى له احدهما جاز لان التوكيل مطلق فيجرى على اطلاقه وقد لا يتفق الجمع بينهما فى البيع الا فيما لا يتغابن الناس فيه لانه توكيل بالشراء وهذا كله بالاجماع

ترجمہ اور اگر ایک شخص نے کسی آدمی کو اس بات کا حکم دیا کہ وہ اس کے واسطے دو معین غلام خریدے اور اس آدمی کے سامنے شمن بیان نہیں کیا پھر اس آدمی نے مؤکل کیلئے ان دونوں میں سے ایک غلام خریدا تو جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ تو کیل مطلق ہے۔ لہذاوہ اپنے اطلاق پر جاری ہوگی اور کبھی بیچ میں دونوں غلاموں کا جمع کرنا ممکن نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ مگر اس صورت میں جس میں لوگ دھوکہ نہ کھاتے ہوں ۔ اسلئے کہ یہ توکیل بالشراء ہے اور یہ تمام کا تمام بالا جماع ہے۔
He said: And whoever commands a man to buy for him two specified slaves and does not name a price for him, then he buys one of them for him, it is valid, because the agency is absolute, so it is executed according to its absoluteness, and sometimes combining both in one sale may not be possible, except in cases where people are not deceived, because it is an agency to buy. And all of this is by consensus. Translation: And if a person commanded another man to buy for him two specified slaves and did not name a price, then that man bought one of the two slaves for the principal, it is valid. Because the agency is absolute, so it will operate according to its absoluteness, and sometimes it is not possible to combine both in a purchase, except in a situation where people are not deceived. Because this is an agency to buy, and all of this is by consensus.
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This is a concise matn (memorization anchor) from the Hanafi school stating a ruling on absolute agency. The scenario: a principal tells an agent to buy two specified slaves without specifying a price. The agent buys only one of them. The ruling: this is valid because the agency is absolute (mutaqliq) – the principal did not restrict the agent to buy both together or separately. The agent may buy one or both as he sees fit, provided the price is fair (no taghabun, i.e., no gross deception). The text notes that the combined purchase of both may not always be feasible (e.g., seller may not sell both together), so buying one is permissible. This ruling is by consensus (ijma') among the Hanafi imams. The commentary explains the reasoning: the absolute authorization allows flexibility, and the condition of no deception ensures the principal is not harmed.

Key Terms
توكيل مطلق (tawkeel mutlaq): An agency without restrictions on time, manner, or number of purchases; the agent has discretion.
تغابن (taghabun): Deception or gross inequality in a transaction; if present, the sale may be invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Invalid because the agency was for both
B) Valid only if the price is named
C) Valid because the agency is absolute
D) Valid only if both are bought together

Key Takeaway
An absolute agency allows the agent to buy one of two specified items, subject to fair pricing, and this ruling is established by ijma.

Overpayment by an Agent: Strict Limits vs. Customary Tolerance
Intra-Hanafi dispute on overpayment in agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between Imam Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's positions on an agent overpaying for one item in a total-price purchase and apply the ruling to a similar scenario.

Core Idea
An agent's overpayment binds the principal only if it is minor and customary, per Sahibayn; Abu Hanifa requires strict adherence to implied limits.

This lesson examines a classical Hanafi dispute in the law of agency (wakala). When a principal instructs an agent to buy two items of equal value for a lump sum, does an overpayment on one item automatically make the agent liable? Imam Abu Hanifa and his two leading disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), offer different answers based on the interplay of explicit speech, implicit indication, and custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Legal Issue
Read the passage and pinpoint the exact scenario: an agent is appointed to buy two slaves of equal value for 1000 rupees. He buys one slave for more than 500 rupees. The central question: Is the purchase binding on the principal?
The principal's instruction implicitly sets a per-slave price of 500.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's Position and Proof
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that any excess over 500, whether small or large, makes the purchase non-binding on the principal; the agent is liable. His proof: the principal's implicit indication (dalala) divides the total equally, and explicit speech (sarih) takes precedence when possible.
An exception (istihsan) exists if the agent later buys the second slave within the budget before a dispute.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Proof
The Sahibayn argue that the agency is absolute but restricted by custom (muta'arif). If the overpayment is within the range of what people commonly overlook (ghabn yasir) and enough money remains to buy the other slave, the purchase binds the principal. Only ghabn fahish (excessive overpayment) makes it non-binding.
Custom (urf) is a secondary source in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Compare the Thamarah al-Khilaf and Identify the Mufta Bihi
The practical difference: Under Abu Hanifa, any overpayment — even 1 rupee — makes the agent liable unless he later completes the objective. Under the Sahibayn, minor fluctuations tolerated by custom are allowed. Later Hanafi fatwa prefers the Sahibayn's view.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf; both views are part of the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الوكالة
صریح دلالت سے بڑھ کر ہے۔
اشرف الہدایہ جلد دہم

تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر ایک شخص نے دوسرے شخص کو ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض ایسے دو غلام خریدنے کا وکیل کیا جن کی قیمت بھی برابر ہے۔ تو حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ نے فرمایا کہ اگر وکیل نے ان دونوں غلاموں میں سے ایک غلام کو پانچ سورو پسیہ یا پانچ سورو پیر سے کم کے عوض خریدا تو یہ خریداری جائز ہوگی ۔ اور مؤکل پر نافذ ہوگی ۔ لیکن اگر پانچ سو روپیہ سے زائد کے عوض خریدا تو یہ خریداری مؤکل پر لازم نہ ہوگی ۔ بلکہ وکیل پر لازم ہوگی ۔ پانچ سورو پہیہ سے زائد کی مقدار کم ہو یا زیادہ ہو۔ دلیل اس کی یہ ہے کہ موکل نے ایک ہزار روپیہ دونوں غلاموں کا مقابل قرار دیا ہے۔ اور دونوں غلاموں کی قیمت برابر ہے۔ لہذا دلالہ یہ بات ثابت ہو گئی کہ ایک ہزار روپیہ دونوں غلاموں کے درمیان آدھا آدھا تقسیم ہو گا گویا مؤکل نے دونوں غلاموں میں سے ہر ایک غلام کو پانچ سو روپیہ کے عوض خرید کرنے کا وکیل کیا ہے۔ پس اگر وکیل نے ایک غلام پانچ سوروپیہ کے عوض خریدا تو یہ خریداری موکل کے حکم کے عین موافق ہوگی اور جب یہ خریداری مؤکل کے حکم کے موافق ہے تو یہ خریداری جائز ہوگی اور مؤکل پر نافذ ہوگی اور اگر وکیل نے ایک غلام پانچ سوروپیہ سے کم کے عوض خریدا تو اس میں بلاشبہ موکل کے حکم کی مخالفت ہے لیکن اس مخالفت میں مؤکل کا فائدہ اور مؤکل کی بھلائی ہے۔ لہذا یہ خریداری بھی جائز ہوگی اور موکل پر نافذ ہو گی۔ ہاں اگر وکیل نے ایک غلام پانچ سو روپیہ سے زائد کے عوض خریدا ده زائد مقدار خواہ کم ہو یا زیادہ ہو تو اس میں موکل کے حکم کی مخالفت ہے اور مؤکل کا نقصان ہے اور جب مؤکل کے حکم کی مخالفت ہے اور اس کا نقصان ہے تو یہ خریداری مؤکل کیلئے جائز نہ ہوگی۔ یعنی اس خریداری کا ذمہ دار وکیل ہوگا۔ متوکل ہرگز ذمہ دار نہ ہو گا۔ لیکن اگر وکیل نے ایک غلام پانچ سو روپیہ سے زائد مثلاً چھ سو روپیہ کے عوض خریدا اور پھر وکیل اور مؤکل کے درمیان خصومت پیش آنے سے پہلے پہلے وکیل نے دوسر ا غلام ایک ہزار میں سے باقی رقم یعنی چار سو روپیہ کے عوض خرید لیا تو یہ خریداری بھی استحسانا جائز ہوگی اور موکل پر لازم اور نافذ ہو گی ۔ اگر چہ قیاس کا تقاضہ یہ ہے کہ اس صورت میں بھی مذکورہ خریداری مؤکل پر لازم نہ ہو گی ۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں بھی مؤکل کی مخالفت موجود ہے۔ اور جب مؤکل کی مخالفت موجود ہے تو مذکورہ خریداری مؤکل پر نافذ نہ ہوگی۔ قیاس ہی کے مطابق امام مالک، امام شافعی ، امام احمد کا قول ہے۔
وجہ استحسان یہ ہے کہ پہلا غلام جس کو چھ سو روپیہ کے عوض خریدا تھا اس کا خریدنا ابھی تک قائم ہے خصومت کی وجہ سے اس کا حال متغیر نہیں ہوا۔ پھر جب وکیل نے دوسرا غلام چار سو روپیہ کے عوض خریدا تو موکل وہ غرض جس کو اس نے صراحہ بیان کیا تھا کہ دونوں غلام ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض خریدے جائیں حاصل ہو گئی اور ایک ہزار روپیہ کا دونوں غلاموں پر برابر منقسم ہونا موکل کے کلام سے دلالہ ثابت ہے۔
حاصل یہ کہ مؤکل کا صریح کلام تو یہ ہے کہ دونوں غلام ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض حاصل ہوں اور دلالیہ اس کے کلام سے معلوم ہوتا ہے اس نے ہر غلام کا عوض پانچ سو روپیہ قرار دیا ہے اور قاعدہ یہ ہے کہ اگر کلام صریح پر عمل کرنا ممکن ہو تو صریح دلالت سے بڑھ کر ہوتا ہے یعنی جب صراحت موجود ہو اور اس پر عمل کرنا بھی ممکن ہو تو دلالت باطل ہو جاتی ہے اس کا اعتبار نہیں کیا جاتا ہے۔ پس چونکہ یہاں دونوں غلاموں کی خریداری مؤکل کی غرض کے موافق ہے اسلئے یہ خریداری جائز ہوگی۔ اور موکل پر نافذ ہوگی۔

اشرف الہدایہ جلد - و ہم
۲۲۷
صاحبین کا نقطہ نظر
كتاب الوكالة
وقال ابويوسف ومحمد ان اشترى احدهما بأكثر من نصف الالف بما يتغابن الناس فيه وقد بقى من الالف ما يشترى بمثله الباقى جاز لان التوكيل مطلق لكنه يتقيد بالمتعارف وهو فيما قلنا ولكن لابد ان يبقى من الالف باقية يشترى بمثلها البـاقـي ليــمــكــد ه تحصيل غرض الأمر ترجمہ اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ اگر وکیل نے ان دونوں غلاموں میں سے ایک غلام کو ایک ہزار کے آدھے سے اس قدر زائد کے عوض خریدا جس میں عام طور سے لوگ دھو کہ کھا جاتے ہیں اور ایک ہزار روپیہ میں سے اس قدر باقی ہے جس کے مثل کے عوض دوسرا غلام خریدا جا سکتا ہے تو یہ جائز ہے اسلئے کہ تو کیل مطلق ہے لیکن وہ متعارف کے ساتھ مقید ہے اور متعارف اس میں ہے جو ہم نے کہا لیکن یہ ضروری ہے کہ ایک ہزار میں سے اس قدر باقی ہو جس کے مثل کے عوض باقی غلام کو خریدا جاسکتا ہوتا کہ وکیل کیلئے مؤکل کی غرض کا حاصل کرنا ممکن ہو ۔ تشریح گذشتہ مسئلہ میں صاحبین کا قول یہ ہے کہ اگر وکیل نے ایک غلام پانچ سو روپیہ سے اس قدر زائد کے عوض خریدا جس میں عام طور سے لوگ دھو کہ کھا جاتے ہیں یعنی نین بیر کے ساتھ خریدا مثلاً پانچ سو روپیہ کی مالیت کا غلام سوا پانچ سورو پیز کے عوض خریدا اور ایک روپیہ میں سے اس قدر ہ تم باقی ہے ( مثلاً پونے پانچ سو روپیہ ) جس کے عوض دوسر اعلام خریدا جا سکتا ہے تو یہ خریداری جائز ہے اور اس کا نفاذ موکل پر ہوگا یعنی یہ خریداری مؤکل کیلئے ہوگی۔
دلیل یہ ہے کہ موکل کی طرف سے یہ تو کیل مطلق ہے یعنی پانچ سو روپیہ کے ساتھ مقید نہیں ہے البتہ متعارف اور مروج کے ساتھ مقید ہے اور رواج اس میں ہے جو ہم نے کہا یعنی نین بیر ۔ مطلب یہ ہے کہ موکل نے دو غلام جن کی قیمت تقریباً برابر، برابر ہے۔ ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض خریدنے کا حکم دیا، گویا یوں کہا کہ ایک غلام لگ بھگ پانچ سو روپیہ کا ہو عرف اور رواج میں اس کا مطلب یہ ہوتا ہے کہ اگر پانچ سو روپیہ سے اس قدر زائد رقم کے عوض خریدا گیا جس میں عام طور سے لوگوں کو دھو کہ ہو جاتا ہے (اسی کو بن میسر کہتے ہیں ) تو اس میں کوئی حرج نہیں ہے یعنی نین بیر کی مقدار زائد رقم ، مؤکل کے حکم کے تحت داخل ہوگی۔ اس کو مؤکل کے حکم کی خلاف ورزی نہیں کہا جائے گا۔ لیکن یہ ضروری ہے کہ ایک ہزار روپیہ میں سے باقی ماندہ رقم اس قدر ہو کہ اس کے عوض دوسر اغلام خریدا جا سکے تاکہ موکل کی غرض کا حاصل کرنا یعنی ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض دو غلاموں کا مالک ہونا ممکن ہو سکے۔
حضرت امام صاحب اور صاحبین کے مذاہب کے درمیان فرق یہ ہوا کہ صاحبین کے نزدیک اگر ایک غلام کو پانچ سوروپیہ سے اس قدر زائد رقم کے عوض خریدا جس کو نین بیر کہا جاتا ہے تو یہ خریداری موکل پر لازم ہوگی اور اگر اس قدر زائد رقم کے عوض خریدا جس کو نین فاحش کہا جاتا ہے تو یہ خریداری موکل پر لازم نہ ہوگی بلکہ وکیل پر لازم ہوگی ۔
اور امام صاحب کے نزدیک پانچ سو روپیہ سے زائد رقم غبن فاحش کی صورت میں ہو یا نین بیر کی صورت میں ہو دونوں صورتوں میں یه خریداری مؤکل پر لازم نہ ہوگی بلکہ وکیل پر لازم ہوگی ۔
Book of Agency. Explicit indication is stronger than implicit indication. Explanation of the issue: If a person appoints another as agent to buy two slaves of equal value for 1000 rupees, Imam Abu Hanifa said: If the agent buys one of the two slaves for 500 rupees or less, the purchase is valid and binding on the principal. But if he buys for more than 500 rupees, the purchase is not binding on the principal; rather it is binding on the agent, whether the excess is small or large. The proof is that the principal made 1000 rupees the equivalent of both slaves, and their values are equal. So it is established implicitly that the 1000 rupees are divided half and half between the two slaves, meaning the principal appointed the agent to buy each slave for 500 rupees. If the agent buys one slave for exactly 500 rupees, it conforms to the principal's command and is valid and binding. If he buys for less than 500, there is opposition but it benefits the principal, so it is still valid and binding. If he buys for more than 500, there is opposition and harm, so it is not valid for the principal; the agent is responsible. However, if the agent bought one slave for more than 500, e.g. 600, and before any dispute arises, he buys the second slave with the remaining 400, then this purchase is valid by istihsan and binding on the principal, although qiyas would require it not to be binding because opposition remains. The view of Malik, Shafi'i, and Ahmad is according to qiyas. The reason for istihsan is that the first purchase is still in place; when the agent buys the second slave for 400, the principal's objective (buying both slaves for 1000) is achieved. The explicit speech of the principal is to get both slaves for 1000, and implicit indication is that each is for 500. The rule is that if the explicit speech can be acted upon, it takes precedence over implicit indication. Since both slaves were bought in accordance with the principal's objective, the purchase is valid and binding. Then the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: If the agent buys one slave for more than half of the 1000 by an amount in which people commonly overlook the difference (ghabn yasir), and enough remains to buy the other slave, it is valid because the agency is absolute but restricted by custom, which is as we said. However, it is necessary that enough of the 1000 remains to buy the other slave so that the agent can achieve the principal's objective. The difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is that according to the Sahibayn, if the agent buys one slave for more than 500 by a small margin (ghabn yasir), it is binding on the principal; if by a large margin (ghabn fahish), it is not binding. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, whether the excess is small or large, it is not binding on the principal.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses a key issue in the law of agency (wakala): when an agent appointed to buy two slaves of equal value for a total price of 1000 rupees purchases one of them for more than 500 rupees. The text presents the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn). Imam Abu Hanifa holds that any amount exceeding 500 rupees, whether small or large, makes the purchase binding on the agent, not the principal, because the principal's implicit instruction set a limit of 500 per slave. An exception arises if the agent later completes the purchase of both slaves within the total budget before any dispute; then, by istihsan (juristic preference), the purchase is valid and binding on the principal, as the principal's explicit objective (both slaves for 1000) is achieved. The Sahibayn differ: they consider the agency as absolute but restricted by custom. If the excess is within the range of what people normally overlook (ghabn yasir, i.e., a minor overpayment), the purchase is valid and binding on the principal, provided enough money remains to buy the second slave. If the excess is excessive (ghabn fahish), it is not binding. The practical difference is that Imam Abu Hanifa is stricter: any overpayment makes the agent liable, while the Sahibayn allow minor deviations that are customary. The passage also contrasts the Hanafi approach with other schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) which follow qiyas (analogy) without the istihsan exception.

Key Terms
وكالة (wakala): A contract by which one person (the principal/muwakkil) appoints another (the agent/wakil) to perform a specific legal act on his behalf.
غبن (ghabn): A discrepancy between the actual value of an item and the price paid; here divided into ghabn yasir (minor, customarily overlooked) and ghabn fahish (excessive).
دلالة (dalala): Implicit indication from speech; here, the principal's statement of a total price for two equal slaves implies each is for half.
متعارف (muta'arif): What is commonly known and accepted in customary practice; here, the customary tolerance for minor overpayment (ghabn yasir).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if an agent appointed to buy two slaves for 1000 rupees buys one slave for 600 rupees and later, before any dispute, buys the second slave for 400 rupees, what is the ruling?
A) The purchase of the first slave is void, and the agent is liable for the loss.
B) Both purchases are valid and binding on the principal by istihsan.
C) Only the second purchase is valid; the first is the agent's responsibility.
D) The principal may accept or reject both purchases.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi agency law, minor customary overpayment is tolerated by the Sahibayn, while Imam Abu Hanifa requires strict adherence to implied limits unless the principal's objective is fully met through istihsan.

intermediate
6 min
Sahibayn's Position on Agent's Purchase with Ghabn Yasir
States Sahibayn's ruling and condition for valid agency purchase exceeding half the amount

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding the agent's purchase exceeding the authorized amount and distinguish when the purchase binds the principal versus the agent.

Core Idea
The Sahibayn allow minor customary overpayment in agency; Abu Hanifa does not.

In this lesson, we examine an intra-Hanafi disagreement about an agent's authority to exceed the specified price. The core issue is whether a minor customary overpayment (ghabn yasir) is included in an absolute agency. We will explore the reasoning of the Sahibayn and why Imam Abu Hanifa holds a stricter view.

Guided Path
1
State the scenario and the ruling
Understand the case: an agent is authorized to buy two slaves for 1000 dirhams. He buys one for more than 500. The dispute focuses on whether the purchase binds the principal when the overpayment is minor and enough money remains for the second slave.
2
Identify the two positions
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that any excess over half the amount invalidates the agency for that transaction, making the agent personally liable. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the purchase binds the principal if the overpayment is ghabn yasir and the remaining funds suffice.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
3
Analyze the Sahibayn's proof
The Sahibayn argue that the agency is absolute (mutlaq) but restricted by custom (mut'ārif). Custom tolerates minor overpayment (ghabn yasir) as part of normal transactions. Therefore, such overpayment is implicitly authorized.
The word mut'ārif is key: it means what is customary.
4
Consider the condition for validity
The Sahibayn add a condition: enough money must remain from the total amount to buy the second slave. This ensures the principal's objective of acquiring both slaves can still be achieved.
If insufficient remains, the purchase does not bind the principal even according to the Sahibayn.
5
Understand the practical difference
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, any excess over the indicated portion violates the authorization, regardless of custom. According to the Sahibayn, only excessive overpayment (ghabn fahish) violates; minor overpayment (ghabn yasir) is included. The mufta bihi view for practical ease often follows the Sahibayn.
This dispute does not affect the validity of the sale itself, only whom it binds.

غبن فاحش کی صورت میں ہو یا نین بیر کی صورت میں ہو دونوں صورتوں میں یه خریداری مؤکل پر لازم نہ ہوگی بلکہ وکیل پر لازم ہوگی ۔
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: If he buys one of them for more than half the thousand by an amount in which people customarily deceive each other, and there remains from the thousand enough to buy the other with a similar amount, it is valid because the agency is absolute but restricted by custom, and that is as we said. However, it is necessary that enough remains from the thousand to buy the remaining slave with a similar amount so that the agent can achieve the principal's purpose. Translation and explanation follow. The difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn is that according to the Sahibayn, if a slave is bought for more than five hundred by an amount called ghabn yasir (minor deception), the purchase is binding on the principal; but if bought by an amount called ghabn fahish (major deception), it is not binding on the principal but on the agent. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, whether the excess is ghabn fahish or ghabn yasir, in both cases the purchase is not binding on the principal but on the agent.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding an agent's purchase of one of two slaves for more than half the authorized amount. The author first states the position of the Sahibayn: if the agent buys one slave for more than 500 dirhams by an amount that is within the customary range of mutual deception (ghabn yasir, e.g., 525 dirhams for a 500-dirham slave) and the remaining money is still sufficient to buy the second slave (e.g., 475 dirhams left), then the purchase is valid and binding on the principal. The reason is that the agency is unrestricted (mutlaq) but is restricted by custom (mut'ārif), which includes a degree of tolerable overpayment. However, the condition that enough money remains ensures the principal's objective of acquiring both slaves can still be met. The author then explains the difference: for the Sahibayn, only ghabn fahish (excessive overpayment) makes the purchase non-binding on the principal; ghabn yasir is included in the authorization. For Imam Abu Hanifa, any excess over half the thousand invalidates the agency for that transaction, and the purchase falls on the agent personally.

Key Terms
غبن یسیر (ghabn yasir): A minor overpayment in a transaction that is customary and tolerated by people; not considered excessive deception.
غبن فاحش (ghabn fahish): An excessive overpayment that is not customary; considered a major deception that invalidates the agent's authority to bind the principal.
متعارف (mut'ārif): What is customary and recognized in practice; 'urf that restricts the absolute agency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what condition is necessary for an agent's purchase of one slave at more than half the authorized amount to be valid and binding on the principal?
A) The overpayment must be less than ten dirhams.
B) The overpayment must be within the range of ghabn yasir and the remaining money must suffice to buy the other slave.
C) The agent must have explicit written permission to exceed the amount.
D) The principal must have previously allowed such a purchase in a similar case.

Key Takeaway
The Sahibayn permit minor customary overpayment (ghabn yasir) in an absolute agency if the principal's overall objective remains achievable, while Imam Abu Hanifa holds that any excess over the indicated amount makes the agent personally liable.

Creditor's Instruction to Debtor to Buy a Specific Slave
Ruling on creditor instructing debtor to buy a specific slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a creditor instructing a debtor to buy a specific slave and apply the analogy that specifying the mabi' specifies the seller.

Core Idea
Specifying the sold item in agency implies specifying the seller, making the purchase valid and binding on the principal.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from Al-Hidayah: a creditor tells his debtor to buy a particular slave using the debt money. The purchase is valid and binding on the creditor, even if the slave perishes before delivery. The reasoning hinges on a clear analogy (qiyas jaliy) between specifying the sold item and specifying the seller. Understanding this ruling clarifies how far agency can extend in debt transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the legal act? The creditor (principal) instructs the debtor (agent) to buy a specific slave. The ruling: the purchase is valid and binding on the creditor.
The ruling applies whether or not the creditor takes possession.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas jaliy: the case of specifying the sold item (mabi') is analogized to the case of specifying the seller (ba'i'), which is known to be valid.
The direct dalil is not a text but an analogical extension from an established rule.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does specifying the mabi' lead to specifying the ba'i'? Because a specific slave has only one owner, so naming the slave necessarily identifies the seller. Since specifying the seller is valid, specifying the sold item is valid by analogy.
The effective cause (illa) is the implied identification of the counterparty.
4
Recognize the practical consequence
The purchase is binding on the creditor from the moment of contract. Any loss before delivery falls on the creditor, not the debtor-agent.
This distinguishes it from a general agency where the agent might be liable.
5
Evaluate the strength of the analogy
The analogy is considered jaliy (clear) because the link between specifying the item and specifying the seller is direct and necessary. The ruling is not overridden by istihsan here.
Some other schools might require explicit naming of the seller, but Hanafis accept this indirect specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن له على آخر الف درهم فامره بان يشترى بها هذا العبد فاشتراه جاز لان في تعيين المبيع تعيين البائع ولو عين البائع يجوز على ما نذكره ان شاء الله تعالى ترجمہ اور اگر اس شخص نے جس کا دوسرے پر ایک ہزار روپیہ قرض ہو، قرضدار کو حکم دیا کہ وہ ایک ہزار روپیہ کے عوض یہ غلام خرید لے پس قرضدار نے اس کو خرید لیا تو یہ جائز ہے۔ اسلئے کہ مبیع متعین کرنے میں بائع کی بھی تعین ہو جاتی ہے اور اگر بائع کو متعین کرتا تو جائز ہو جاتا۔ چنانچہ ہم اس کو انشاء اللہ ذکر کریں گے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ ایک شخص کا دوسرے پر ایک ہزار روپیہ قرضہ ہے۔ پس قرض خواہ نے قرضدار کوحکم دیا کہ اس ایک ہزار کے عوض فلاں معین غلام کو خرید لے۔ چنانچہ قرضدار نے اس معینہ غلام کو خرید لیا تو یہ خریداری مؤکل پر لازم ہوگی ۔ خواہ مؤکل اس غلام پر قبضہ کرے خواہ وہ غلام مؤکل کے قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے وکیل کے قبضہ میں مرجائے۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہاں موکل نے مبیع یعنی غلام کو متعین کیا ہے اور مبیع کو متعین کرنے سے بائع بھی متعین ہو جاتا ہے اور مؤکل اگر بائع کو متعین کر دے تو یہ جائز ہے جیسا کہ آئندہ مذکور ہو گا۔ پس اسی طرح طبیعیع کو متعین کرنے کی صورت میں بھی بالا تفاق جائز ہوگا۔
He said: And whoever has a debt of one thousand dirhams on another, and he instructs him to buy this slave with that amount, then he buys it, it is valid because specifying the sold item specifies the seller. And if he specified the seller, it would be valid, as we will mention, Allah willing. Translation: If a creditor tells a debtor to buy a specific slave with the debt, and the debtor buys it, it is valid. The reason is that specifying the sold item results in specifying the seller, and if he specified the seller it would be valid. Explanation: The creditor instructs the debtor to buy a specific slave with the debt. The purchase is binding on the principal (creditor) regardless of whether he takes possession or the slave dies in the agent's hand before possession. The proof is that specifying the sold item specifies the seller, and specifying the seller is valid by consensus, as will be mentioned.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling: if a creditor (principal) instructs his debtor (agent) to buy a specific slave using the debt amount, the purchase is valid and binding on the creditor. The reasoning is that by specifying the slave, the seller is effectively specified because the only seller of that specific slave is its owner. Since the creditor could have directly authorized the debtor to buy from a particular seller, and that is valid, specifying the sold item similarly specifies the seller. The author emphasizes that the purchase is binding on the creditor even if the slave dies in the debtor's hands before delivery to the creditor, because the debtor is acting as an agent and the loss is from the creditor's property once the sale is concluded. This is based on the principle that specifying the sold item (mabi') implies specifying the seller, and the agency is valid thereby.

Key Terms
مبیع (mabi'): The sold item; in this context, a specific slave designated by the principal.
بائع (ba'i'): The seller; the owner of the specified slave, who is implied by specifying the slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Because the debtor is the owner of the debt.
B) Because specifying the sold item specifies the seller, and specifying the seller is valid.
C) Because the creditor gave general permission.
D) Because the slave was already owned by the creditor.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, specifying the subject matter in an agency implies specification of the counterparty, making the purchase valid and binding on the principal.
