Ta'ayyun of Currency in Agency: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ta'ayyun in agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on ta'ayyun of dirhams and dinars in agency and apply the dispute to determine who bears the risk when an agent buys an unspecified item.

Core Idea
Currency becomes specified in agency, altering liability for the agent's purchases.

This passage presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute about whether dirhams and dinars become specific (ta'ayyun) in agency contracts. The case involves a creditor instructing a debtor to buy an unspecified slave with a debt. The answer determines who bears the loss if the slave dies before delivery. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that currency becomes specified in agency, making the purchase non-binding on the principal until he takes possession. The Sahibayn disagree, arguing currency does not become specified, so the purchase binds the principal immediately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the dispute
State the scenario: a creditor instructs a debtor to buy an unspecified slave with the debt amount. The dispute is whether the purchase binds the creditor (principal) or remains the debtor's (agent's) risk.
The key variable is whether the debt amount becomes specified in the agent's hands.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and dalil
Abu Hanifa rules the purchase is not binding on the principal; the agent bears loss if the slave dies before principal takes possession. His proof: dirhams and dinars become specified in agency, as shown by restrictions voiding the agency upon destruction or waiver of the specified item.
Specification makes it akin to transferring a debt without authorization, which is invalid.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and dalil
The Sahibayn rule the purchase binds the principal when the agent takes possession. Their proof: currency does not become specified in exchange transactions, so the agency is unrestricted. Additionally, the principle 'yad al-wakil ka-yad al-muwakkil' makes the agent's possession equivalent to the principal's.
They rely on the fungible nature of currency and the agency maxim.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the slave dies before principal takes possession, Abu Hanifa says it is the agent's loss (debt remains unpaid), while the Sahibayn say it is the principal's loss (debt discharged).
This determines risk allocation in agency transactions.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text does not give a definitive tarjih. Later Hanafi works often adopt the Sahibayn's view for ease of transactions, but both positions are valid.
Knowing both positions is essential for a mufti.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان امره ان یشتری بها عبدا بغير عينه فاشتراه فمات فى يده قبل ان يقبضه الآمر مات من مال المشترى وان قبضه الآمر فهو له وهذا عند ابي حنيفة رضى الله تعالى عنه وقالا هو لازم للآمر اذا قبضه المامور وعلى هذا اذا امره ان يسلم ما عليه او يصرف ما عليه لهما ان الدراهم والدنانير لا تتعينان في المعاوضات دينا كانت او عينا الا ترى انه لو تبايعا عينا بدين ثم تصادقا ان لا دين لا يبطل العقد فصار الاطلاق والتقييد فيه سواء فيصح التوكيل ويلزم الأمر لان يد الوكيل كيده ولابي حنيفة رضى الله عنه انها تتعين في الوكالات الا ترى انه لو قيد الوكالة بالعين منها او بالدين منها ثم استهلك العين او اسقط الدين بطلت الوكالة فاذا تعينت كان هذا تملیک الدین من غير من عليه الدين من غير ان يوكله بقبضه وذالك لا يجوز كما اذا اشتری بدين على غير المشترى او يكون امرا بصرف ما لا يملكه الا بالقبض قبله وذالك باطل كما اذا قال اعط مالی علیک من شئت بخلاف ما اذا عين البائع لانه يصير وكيلا عنه في القبض ثم يتملكه وبخلاف ما اذا امره بالتصدق لانه جعل المال لله تعالى وهو معلوم واذا لم يصح التوكيل نفذ الشرى على المامور فيهلك من ماله الا اذا قبضه الآمر منه لانعقاد البيع تعاطيا
And if he instructs him to buy a servant without specifying it, and he buys it, and it dies in his hand before the principal takes possession, it dies from the property of the buyer. And if the principal takes possession, it is his. This is according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah be pleased with him. And the two (Sahibayn) said: It is binding on the principal if the agent takes possession. And on this, if he instructs him to hand over what is due or to spend what is due, according to them, dirhams and dinars are not specified in exchange transactions, whether as debt or as specific items. Do you not see that if they exchange a specific item for a debt, then they agree that there is no debt, the contract is not invalidated? So the unrestricted and the restricted in that are equal. Thus, agency is valid and binds the principal because the hand of the agent is like his hand. And according to Abu Hanifa, they become specified in agencies. Do you not see that if he restricted the agency to the specific item or to the debt, then the specific item was destroyed or the debt was waived, the agency becomes void? So if they become specified, then this is transferring a debt from the one who owes it to another without authorizing him to collect it, and that is not allowed, just as if he bought with a debt payable by someone other than the seller, or it is an instruction to spend what he does not own except after taking possession, and that is invalid, just as if he said: 'Give what I have with you to whomever you wish.' Unlike the case where he specified the seller, because he becomes his agent in taking possession and then owns it; and unlike the case where he instructs him to give charity, because he makes the property for Allah and it is known. And if the agency is not valid, the purchase is executed on the agent and is lost from his property, unless the principal takes possession from him because the sale was concluded by mutual delivery (ta'ati).
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding when a creditor instructs the debtor to buy an unspecified slave with the debt amount. Imam Abu Hanifa's position: If the debtor buys an unspecified slave, the purchase is not binding on the creditor; the slave remains the debtor's property, and if it dies in his hands before the creditor takes possession, it is from the debtor's property. Only if the creditor takes possession does it become his. The Sahibayn say: The purchase is binding on the creditor immediately when the debtor takes possession, because the agent's hand is like the principal's hand. The reasoning for the Sahibayn: Dirhams and dinars do not become specified in exchange transactions, whether they are debt or specific items. Therefore, the agency is unrestricted and the purchase binds the principal. Imam Abu Hanifa responds: Dirhams and dinars do become specified in agency contracts. Evidence: If the principal restricts the agency to a specific current coin (like a specific dinar) and that coin is destroyed, the agency becomes void. Similarly, if he restricts to a specific debt and that debt is waived, the agency becomes void. This shows they become specified. If they become specified, then the instruction to buy without specifying the slave effectively means transferring a debt (the thousand) from the debtor to someone else (the seller) without authorizing the agent to collect it, which is invalid. Alternatively, it would be an instruction to spend something (the debt) that the creditor does not yet possess, which is invalid like telling someone to give his money to anyone he wishes. The Sahibayn's analogy to charity is distinguished because in charity, the money is made for Allah and is known. The passage concludes that if the agency is invalid, the purchase binds the agent personally unless the creditor takes possession, in which case the sale is valid by ta'ati (mutual delivery).

Key Terms
تعین (ta'ayyun): The process by which dirhams and dinars become specific and non-fungible in the context of agency, according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
ید الوكیل كید الموكل (yad al-wakil ka-yad al-muwakkil): The principle that the agent's hand is like the principal's hand, meaning possession by the agent is equivalent to possession by the principal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the key point of dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn in this passage regarding the purchase of an unspecified slave?
A) Whether the agent can buy without permission.
B) Whether dirhams and dinars become specified (ta'ayyun) in agency contracts.
C) Whether the debtor must first own the slave.
D) Whether the sale must be conducted in a market.

Key Takeaway
The ta'ayyun of currency in agency determines whether the agent's purchase binds the principal or remains the agent's risk.

Agency to Purchase with Debt: The Specification Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agency with unspecified goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of agency to purchase unspecified goods with debt and explain the thamarah al-khilaf between Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Agency with debt for unspecified goods: Abu Hanifa says invalid; Sahibayn says valid; loss allocation differs.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced scenario: a creditor instructs a debtor to purchase a slave using the debt, but the slave is not specified. The ruling depends on whether dirhams and dinars are considered 'specified' in agency contracts. Imam Abu Hanifa and his two disciples disagree, leading to different practical consequences for who bears the risk of loss.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, note the case where the creditor specifies a particular slave – that is valid. Then, the disputed case where no specification is given.
Abu Hanifa and Sahibayn agree on the first scenario but split on the second.
2
Understand Abu Hanifa's reasoning
He argues that dirhams/dinars become specified in agencies, so ordering purchase with a debt transfers ownership of the debt to the agent improperly.
Analogies: restricting agency to a specific currency then consuming it voids the agency.
3
Understand Sahibayn's reasoning
They argue that currency is never specified in contracts, so the agency is valid and the agent's hand represents the principal.
Analogy: if two parties exchange goods for debt and later the debt is cancelled, the contract remains valid.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if the slave perishes before the principal takes possession, who bears the loss? Abu Hanifa: the debtor; Sahibayn: the creditor.
Later school follows Sahibayn for facility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ومن له على آخر الف درهم فامره بان يشترى بها هذا العبد فاشتراه جاز لان في تعيين المبيع تعيين البائع ولو عين البائع يجوز على ما نذكره ان شاء الله تعالى
وان امره ان يشتري بها عبدا بغير عينه فاشتراه فمات فى يده قبل ان يقبضه الآمر مات من مال المشترى وان قبضه الآمر فهو له وهذا عند ابي حنيفة رضى الله تعالى عنه وقالا هو لازم للآمر اذا قبضه المامور وعلى هذا اذا امره ان يسلم ما عليه او يصرف ما عليه لهما ان الدراهم والدنانير لا تتعينان في المعاوضات دينا كانت او عينا الا ترى انه لو تبايعا عينا بدين ثم تصادقا ان لا دين لا يبطل العقد فصار الاطلاق والتقييد فيه سواء فيصح التوكيل ويلزم الأمر لان يد الوكيل كيده ولابي حنيفة رضى الله عنه انها تتعين في الوكالات الا ترى انه لو قيد الوكالة بالعين منها او بالدين منها ثم استهلك العين او اسقط الدين بطلت الوكالة فاذا تعينت كان هذا تمليك الدين من غير من عليه الدين من غير ان يوكله بقبضه وذالك لا يجوز كما اذا اشترى بدين على غير المشترى او يكون امرا بصرف ما لا يملكه الا بالقبض قبله وذالك باطل كما اذا قال اعط مالي عليك من شئت بخلاف ما اذا عين البائع لانه يصير وكيلا عنه في القبض ثم يتملكه وبخلاف ما اذا امره بالتصدق لانه جعل المال لله تعالى وهو معلوم واذا لم يصح التوكيل نفذ الشراء على المأمور فيهلك من ماله الا اذا قبضه الآمر منه لانعقاد البيع تعاطيا
He said: And whoever has upon another a thousand dirhams, and he orders him to buy with it this slave, and he buys him, it is permissible because in specifying the sold item there is specification of the seller. And if he specified the seller, it would be permissible as we will mention, God willing. And if he orders him to buy with it a slave without specifying him, and he buys him, then he dies in his hand before the commander takes possession, he died from the property of the buyer. And if the commander takes possession, it is for him. This is according to Abu Hanifa, may God be pleased with him. And the two said: It is binding upon the commander when the commanded one takes possession. And upon this, if he orders him to deliver what is upon him or to exchange what is upon him, according to them, dirhams and dinars are not specified in commutative contracts, whether as debt or as specific goods. Do you not see that if two people exchange a specific good for a debt, then they agree that there is no debt, the contract is not invalidated? So generality and restriction are equal in it, so the agency is valid and the matter is binding, because the hand of the agent is like his hand. And for Abu Hanifa, may God be pleased with him, they are specified in agencies. Do you not see that if he restricted the agency to a specific good from them or to a debt from them, then he consumed the specific good or cancelled the debt, the agency is invalidated? So when they are specified, this is transferring ownership of the debt to someone other than the one upon whom the debt is owed, without him appointing him as agent for its receipt, and that is not permissible, like if he bought with a debt upon someone other than the buyer, or it is an order to spend what he does not own except by taking possession before it, and that is invalid, like when he said: Give my wealth upon you to whomever you wish; unlike when he specified the seller, because he becomes an agent for him in receipt, then he owns it; and unlike when he ordered him to give charity, because he made the wealth for God Almighty, and it is known. And if the agency is not valid, the purchase takes effect upon the commanded one, and it perishes from his wealth, unless the commander takes possession from him, because the contract is concluded by mutual delivery (ta'ati).
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah discusses the ruling on a creditor ordering a debtor to purchase a slave using the debt amount. The author begins by stating the case where the creditor specifies a particular slave: the purchase is valid and binding on the creditor (the muwakkil). This is because specifying the goods (mabi') effectively specifies the seller (ba'i'), and if the creditor had directly specified the seller, the agency would be valid. The proof is that the creditor's specification of the item makes the transaction clear and eliminates ambiguity, so the debtor acts as an agent in a valid sale.

The passage then presents the core dispute: what if the creditor orders the debtor to buy an unspecified slave? Abu Hanifa holds that such an agency is not valid because dirhams and dinars (the currency of the debt) become specified in the context of agency, even though they are not specified in commutative contracts. His evidence is that if the creditor had restricted the agency to a specific currency or debt, then consuming that currency or cancelling the debt would terminate the agency, showing that the currency is effectively specified. If the currency is thus specified, then the agent (debtor) would be transferring ownership of the debt to himself, which is invalid without proper authorization to receive it. Therefore, the purchase is not binding on the creditor; the slave belongs to the debtor (agent) unless the creditor later takes possession, in which case a new sale by mutual delivery (ta'ati) occurs.

The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, known as Sahibayn) disagree. They argue that dirhams and dinars are not specified in any commutative contract, whether as a debt or as a specific good. They reason that if two parties exchange a specific good for a debt and later agree there was no debt, the contract remains valid, showing that generality and specification are equivalent. Therefore, the agency is valid and the purchase is binding on the creditor once the agent takes possession, because the agent's hand is like the creditor's hand. The creditor becomes the owner of the slave, and the debt is discharged.

The passage also extends this principle to other commands, such as ordering the debtor to deliver or exchange what is owed. The same disagreement applies. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that according to Abu Hanifa, the debtor bears the loss if the slave perishes before creditor takes possession; according to Sahibayn, the creditor bears it once the agent takes possession. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school is that of Imam Muhammad (one of the Sahibayn), as later scholars prefer the view that agency in such cases is valid.

This lesson guards against the misunderstanding that any order to purchase with a debt creates a valid agency; specification of the goods or the seller is crucial for validity according to Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms
توكيل (tawkil): Appointment of an agent to perform a legal act on one's behalf.
مؤكل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints the agent.
وكيل (wakil): The agent appointed by the principal.
تعيين (ta'yin): Specification of a particular item or person, contrasted with generality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a creditor orders a debtor to buy an unspecified slave with the debt, and the slave dies in the debtor's hand before the creditor takes possession, who bears the loss?
A) The creditor bears the loss, and the debt is discharged.
B) The debtor bears the loss, and the debt remains.
C) Both share the loss equally.
D) The slave's seller bears the loss.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that agency to purchase unspecified goods with debt is valid (Sahibayn's view, the mufta bihi), but Abu Hanifa's view makes it invalid with the debtor liable for loss until the creditor takes possession.

10 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Agency to Buy with a Debt
Intra-Hanafi dispute on agency validity and risk allocation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the validity of a creditor appointing his debtor as agent to purchase using the debt as the price, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of each position.

Core Idea
In agency, Abu Hanifa holds that coins become specified, invalidating the agency; the Sahibayn disagree, making the purchase binding on the principal.

This passage examines a crucial difference within the Hanafi school regarding agency when a creditor instructs his debtor to use the debt as the price of a slave. The dispute turns on whether dirhams and dinars become specified in agency contracts, affecting who bears the risk if the purchased item perishes before the principal takes possession. The Sahibayn's view is generally preferred in transactions, but understanding Abu Hanifa's reasoning is essential for grasping Hanafi agency theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
State the case: a creditor (muwakkil) instructs his debtor (wakil) to buy a slave in exchange for the debt. The slave is unspecified, so the seller is also unspecified. The slave dies in the debtor's possession before the creditor takes possession.
The dispute applies only when the creditor does not specify the seller; if specified, agency is valid by consensus.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and proof
Abu Hanifa: The agency is invalid because dirhams/dinars become specified in agency contracts. Proof: If the agency is restricted to a specific number of coins or to the debt, and that specific thing perishes or the debt is remitted, the agency becomes void. Therefore, specifying the debt makes the agency void; the purchase is on the debtor himself.
Abu Hanifa distinguishes between exchange contracts (where coins are not specified) and agency contracts (where they are specified).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: The agency is valid because dirhams/dinars are not specified by specification in exchange contracts, whether debt or available. Their proof: If two people sell a specific asset in exchange for a debt, then later agree there was no debt, the contract is not void, showing the debt was not specified. Thus, general and specific expressions are equal; the agency is valid, and the agent's possession is like the principal's possession.
Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad also hold this view.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: According to Abu Hanifa, if the slave dies in the agent's possession before the principal takes possession, the debtor bears the loss and the debt remains. According to the Sahibayn, the debtor is discharged from the debt as soon as he takes possession of the slave; the loss is on the creditor even if the slave perishes before the creditor's possession.
This shows how the ruling affects risk allocation.
5
Note the consensus qualification
The dispute exists only when the creditor does not specify the seller or counterparty. If the seller is specified, the agency is valid by consensus, and the purchase is binding on the principal in all positions.
This limits the scope of the ikhtilaf.

كتاب الوكالة
(مؤکل) کے قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے وہ غلام قرضدار (وکیل) کے قبضہ میں مرگیا تو وہ مشتری ( قرضدار ) کے مال سے مرا ہے۔ اور اگر موکل نے اس پر قبضہ کر لیا ہوتو وہ موکل کیلئے ہے اور یہ حکم امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک ہے اور صاحبین نے فرمایا کہ موکل کے ذمہ لازم ہوگا۔ جبکہ وکیل نے اس پر قبضہ کر لیا ہو اور اسی اختلاف پر یہ ہے کہ جب قرض خواہ نے قرضدار کو حکم دیا کہ جو کچھ تجھ پر ہے اس کی بیع سلم کرلے یا بیع صرف کر لے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ معاوضات میں دراہم و دنانیر متعین کرنے سے متعین نہیں ہوتے ہیں۔ خواہ مین ہو یا دین ہو۔ کیا تم نہیں دیکھتے ہو کہ اگر دو شخصوں نے ایک مال عین کو قرضہ کے عوض بیع کیا پھر دونوں نے اتفاق کیا کہ کوئی قرضہ نہیں تھا تو عقد باطل نہیں ہوگا۔ پس اس معاملہ میں اطلاق اور تقیید دونوں برابر ہو گئے ۔ لہذا توکیل صحیح ہوگی۔ اور عقد مؤکل کے ذمہ لازم ہوگا کیونکہ وکیل کا قبضہ موکل کے قبضہ کے مانند ہے اور امام ابو حنیفہ کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ دراہم و دنانیر وکالتوں میں متعین ہوتے ہیں۔ کیا تم نہیں دیکھتے ہو کہ اگر دراہم و دنانیر میں سے تین کے ساتھ مقید کیا ہے یا دراہم و دنانیر میں سے قرضہ کے ساتھ مقید کیا ہے پھر معین کو ہلاک کر دیا یا قرضہ ساقط کر دیا تو وکالت باطل ہو جاتی ہے پس جب دراہم و دنانیر (وکالت ) میں متعین ہوئے تو یہ قرضدار کے علاوہ کو قرضہ کا مالک کرنا ہوا بغیر اسکے کہ اس کو قبضہ کرنے کا وکیل کرے حالانکہ یہ بات جائز نہیں ہے۔ جیسا کہ ایک شخص نے ایسے قرضہ کے عوض خریداری کی جو قرضہ مشتری کے علاوہ پر ہے یا ایسے مال کے صرف کرنے کا حکم ہو گا۔ جس کا وہ مالک نہیں ہے مگر یہ کہ حکم کرنے سے پہلے قبضہ کرے حالانکہ وہ باطل ہے۔ جیسا کہ جب کہا میرا مال جو تجھ پر ہے تو جس کو چاہے دے دے اسکے برخلاف جب مؤکل نے بائع کو معین کر دیا ہو۔ کیونکہ بائع (اولاً) موکل کی طرف سے قبضہ کرنے کا وکیل ہو جائے گا۔ پھر وہ اس کا مالک ہو جائے گا اور اس کے برخلاف جب مؤکل نے قرضدار کو یہ مال صدقہ کرنے کا حکم دیا ہو۔ اسلئے کہ اس نے مال اللہ تعالیٰ کے واسطے کر دیا اور وہ معلوم ہے اور جب توکیل صحیح نہیں ہوتی تو خریداری وکیل پر نافذ ہوگی اور غلام اس کے مال سے ہلاک ہوگا ۔ مگر جبکہ مؤکل ، وکیل کی طرف سے قبضہ کرے کیونکہ بیع تعاطی منعقد ہو گئی ہے۔ تشریح صورت مسئلہ یہ ہے کہ اگر قرض خواہ نے قرضدار کو حکم دیا کہ وہ ایک ہزار روپیہ جو میرا تجھ پر ہے۔ اس کے عوض ایک غلام خرید کر دے اور غلام متعین نہیں کیا یعنی قرض خواہ نے قرضدار کو اپنے قرضہ کے عوض ایک غیر معین غلام خریدنے کا وکیل کیا اور جب غلام ( مبیع ) غیر معین ہے تو بائع بھی غیر معین ہوگا۔ پس قرضدار نے ایک غلام خریدا مگر اس غلام پر ابھی قرض خواہ ( موکل) نے قبضہ نہیں کیا تھا کہ قرضدار (وکیل) ہی کے قبضہ میں وہ غلام مر گیا تو حضرت امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک یہ غلام مشتری (وکیل) کے مال سے مرے گا اور قرض خواہ یعنی مؤکل کا ایک ہزار روپیہ وکیل یعنی قرضدار پر علی حالہ واجب رہے گا اور اگر موکل یعنی قرض خواہ نے اس غلام پر قبضہ کر لیا اور پھر وہ غلام مرا تو یہ موکل کے مال سے مرے گا۔ یعنی وکیل ( قرضدار) پر جو قرضہ تھا وہ اس کے ذمہ سے ساقط ہو جائے گا اور اس نقصان کا ذمہ دار مؤکل ہو گا۔ مگر یہ اسلئے نہیں کہ یہ وکالت درست ہے بلکہ اسلئے کہ مؤکل کے قبضہ کرنے سے مؤکل اور وکیل کے درمیان بیع تعاطی منعقد ہوگئی ہے اور بیع تعاطی جائز ہے۔ اور صاحبین کے نزدیک حکم یہ ہے کہ اگر وکیل ( قرضدار ) نے غلام پر قبضہ کر لیا تو یہ غلام مؤکل پر لازم ہو گا۔ یعنی مؤکل ( قرض خواہ ) ہی اس کا مالک ہو گا۔ خواہ موکل اس پر قبضہ کرے یا وکیل کے قبضہ میں مر جائے یعنی دونوں صورتوں میں وکیل ( قرضدار ) کے ذمہ سے قرضہ ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اس کے قائل امام شافعی اور امام احمد ہیں۔ حاصل یہ کہ امام صاحب کے نزدیک یہ توکیل درست نہیں ہے اور صاحبین کے نزدیک درست ہے۔ یہی اختلاف اس صورت میں ہے جب قرض خواہ ، قرضدار کو یہ حکم دے کہ میرا ایک ہزار درہم جو تجھ پر ہے تو اس کے عوض کسی شخص سے دس من گندم کی بیع سلم کر لے یا ان ایک ہزار درہم کے عوض بیع صرف کرلے تو امام ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک مسلم فیہ اور بدل صرف پر قرض خواہ (مؤکل) نے قبضہ نہ کیا ہو بلکہ قرضدار (وکیل) کے قبضہ میں ہلاک ہو گئے ہوں تو یہ وکیل ( قرضدار ) کا مال ہلاک ہو گا اور موکل ( قرض خواہ) نے مسلم فیہ اور بدل صرف پر قبضہ کر لیا ہو تو یہ مؤکل ( قرض خواہ) کا مال ہو گیا اب اگر یہ ہلاک ہوا تو موکل کا مال ہلاک ہو گا۔ اور وکیل قرضہ سے سبکدوش ہو جائے گا۔ اور صاحبین کے نزدیک مسلم فیہ اور بدل صرف پر اگر وکیل ( قرضدار) نے قبضہ کر لیا ہو تو وہ موکل کے ذمہ لازم ہوگا خواہ موکل کے قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے وکیل ( قرضدار) کے قبضہ میں ہلاک ہو جائے ۔ لیکن یہ اختلاف اس صورت میں ہے جبکہ قرض خواہ نے مسلم الیہ کو اور اس شخص کو جس کے ساتھ عقد صرف کرنے کا حکم دیا ہے متعین نہ کیا ہو۔ چنانچہ اگر قرض خواہ نے ان کو متعین کر دیا ہو تو یہ توکیل بالاتفاق صحیح ہے اور قرضدار (وکیل) کی خریداری، متوکل ( قرض خواہ) پر لازم ہوگی۔ خواہ مؤکل مسلم فیہ اور بدل صرف پر قبضہ کرے یا موکل کے قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے وکیل ( قرضدار ) کے قبضہ میں ہلاک ہو جائے۔ صاحبین کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ دراہم و دنانیر، بیع وغیرہ عقد معاوضہ میں متعین کرنے سے متعین نہیں ہوتے ، دراہم و دنانیر خواہ دین یعنی کسی کے ذمہ میں ثابت ہوں خواہ عین ہوں یعنی کسی کے ذمہ میں ثابت نہ ہوں بلکہ عقد کرنے والے کے پاس موجود ہوں۔ چنانچہ اگر شاہد نے کہا کہ میرے ایک ہزار درہم ، خالد کے ذمہ قرضہ ہیں ۔ پس خالد قرضدار نے قرضہ کے عوض اپنا مال عین مثلا ایک سو دینار قرض خواہ کے ہاتھ فروخت کر دیے۔ پھر دونوں میں اتفاق ہو گیا کہ شاہد کا خالد پر کوئی قرضہ نہیں تھا تو شاہد اور خالد کے درمیان مذکورہ عقد باطل نہیں ہوگا۔ بلکہ شاہد پر قرضہ کا مثل یعنی ایک ہزار درہم واجب ہوں گے ۔ ملاحظہ فرمائیے اگر دراہم و دنانیر متعین کرنے سے متعین ہو جاتے تو خالد کے ذمہ دین نہ ہونے پر اتفاق کرنے کی صورت میں مذکورہ عقد باطل ہو جاتا کیونکہ عقد میں جن دراہم کو بصورت دین متعین کیا تھا وہ باقی نہیں رہے حالانکہ یہاں عقد باطل نہیں ہوتا ۔ پس ثابت ہوا کہ دراہم و دنانیر دین ہوں یا عین ہوں۔ عقد معاوضہ میں متعین کرنے سے متعین نہیں ہوتے ہیں۔ اور جو چیز متعین کرنے سے متعین نہ ہوتی ہو اس میں اطلاق اور تقیید دونوں برابر ہیں اطلاق کی صورت تو یہ ہے کہ قرض خواہ قرضدار سے یہ کہے کہ میرے واسطے ایک ہزار درہم کے عوض ایک غلام خرید کر لا اور اس ایک ہزار کو اس قرضہ کی طرف منسوب نہ کرے جو قرضہ قرضدار پر واجب ہے۔ اور تقیید کی صورت یہ ہے کہ اس ایک ہزار کو قرضہ کی طرف منسوب کرے مثلاً قرض خواہ یہ کہے کہ میرے لئے ایک غلام اس ایک ہزار کے عوض خرید کر لا۔ جو ایک ہزار میرا تجھ پر واجب ہے۔ پس جب دراہم و دنانیر متعین کرنے سے متعین نہیں ہوتے۔ اور جو چیز متعین کرنے سے متعین نہ ہوتی ہو اس میں اطلاق اور تقیید دونوں برابر ہیں تو قرض خواہ کا اپنے قرضدار کو یہ کہنا کہ تو میرے واسطے ایک غلام اس ایک ہزار درہم کے عوض خرید کر لا جو میرا تجھ پر ہے اور یہ کہنا کہ ایک ہزار درہم کے عوض خرید کر لا۔ دونوں برابر ہیں اور قرض خواہ اگر یہ کہے کہ تو میرے لئے ایک ہزار درہم کے عوض ایک غلام خرید کر لا اور اس ایک ہزار کو قرضہ کی طرف منسوب نہ کرے تو یہ توکیل بالاتفاق درست ہے۔ لہذا اگر ایک ہزار کو قرضہ کی طرف منسوب کر دیا تو اس صورت میں بھی توکیل درست ہوگی ۔ اور جب اس صورت میں توکیل درست ہے تو وکیل یعنی قرضدار کی خریداری مؤکل یعنی قرض خواہ پر لازم ہوگی۔ پس اگر غلام ، قرض خواہ (مؤکل) کے قبضہ کرنے سے پہلے وکیل ( قرضدار) کے قبضہ میں مر گیا تو یہ موکل کے مال سے مرے گا۔ یعنی اس غلام کا مرنا مؤکل ( قرض خواہ) کا نقصان شمار ہوگا نہ
Book of Agency. If the slave dies in the possession of the debtor (agent) before the creditor (principal) takes possession, then it died from the property of the buyer (debtor). And if the principal had taken possession, then it is for the principal. This ruling is according to Imam Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn said: it becomes binding on the principal, once the agent has taken possession. And upon this same difference is when the creditor instructed the debtor: 'Make a salam sale of what you owe me, or make a sarf sale.' The proof of the Sahibayn is that in exchange contracts, dirhams and dinars are not specified by specifying them, whether they are a debt or available. Do you not see that if two persons sold a specific asset in exchange for a debt, then both agreed that there was no debt, the contract would not be void. So in this matter, general and specific are equal. Therefore the agency is valid, and the contract becomes binding on the principal because the possession of the agent is like the possession of the principal. And the proof of Imam Abu Hanifa is that dirhams and dinars become specified in agencies. Do you not see that if it is restricted to three from the dirhams and dinars, or restricted to the debt from the dirhams and dinars, then the specified item is destroyed or the debt is remitted, the agency becomes void. So when dirhams and dinars become specified in agency, this becomes making someone other than the debtor own the debt without appointing him as an agent to take possession, which is not permissible. Like a person purchasing in exchange for a debt that is on someone other than the buyer, or ordering to exchange property that he does not own, unless he takes possession before ordering, which is invalid. As when he said, 'My property that is upon you, give it to whomever you wish,' contrary to when the principal specified the seller. Because the seller (first) becomes an agent of the principal to take possession, then he becomes its owner. And contrary to when the principal ordered the debtor to give this property as charity, because he dedicated it to Allah and it is known. And when the agency is not valid, the purchase is effective on the agent and the slave perishes from his property, except when the principal takes possession from the agent because a contract of mu'atah has been concluded. Explanation of the case: If the creditor ordered the debtor to buy a slave for him in exchange for the thousand rupees that is upon him, and the slave was not specified, i.e., the creditor appointed the debtor as agent to buy an unspecified slave in exchange for his debt. And when the slave is unspecified, the seller is also unspecified. So the debtor bought a slave, but before the creditor (principal) took possession, the slave died in the possession of the debtor (agent). Then according to Imam Abu Hanifa, this slave dies from the property of the buyer (agent), and the creditor's thousand rupees remain due from the debtor. And if the principal (creditor) took possession of the slave and then the slave died, it dies from the principal's property, i.e., the debt on the agent is discharged, and the loss is borne by the principal. But this is not because the agency is valid, but because the possession by the principal constitutes a contract of mu'atah between principal and agent, and mu'atah is valid. According to the Sahibayn, if the agent took possession of the slave, then the slave becomes binding on the principal, i.e., the principal owns it whether he takes possession or it dies in the agent's possession. In both cases, the debt on the agent is discharged. Imam Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad also hold this view. Summary: According to Imam Abu Hanifa, this agency is not valid; according to the Sahibayn, it is valid. This same difference applies when the creditor orders the debtor: 'Make a salam sale of ten maunds of wheat with someone in exchange for the thousand dirhams you owe me' or 'Make a sarf sale in exchange for those thousand dirhams.' According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if the creditor (principal) did not take possession of the salam commodity or the exchange in sarf, but it perished in the possession of the debtor (agent), then it is the agent's property that perished, and the creditor's debt remains. If the principal took possession, then it becomes the principal's property; if it perishes, the loss is on the principal, and the agent is released from the debt. According to the Sahibayn, if the agent took possession of the salam commodity or the exchange in sarf, it becomes binding on the principal, even if it perishes in the agent's possession before the principal takes possession. But this difference applies when the creditor did not specify the person from whom to make the salam or the person with whom to make the sarf. If he specified them, then the agency is valid by consensus, and the purchase of the agent is binding on the principal, whether the principal takes possession or it perishes in the agent's possession before the principal's possession. The proof of the Sahibayn is that dirhams and dinars are not specified by specification in exchange contracts like sale, etc. Dirhams and dinars, whether they are a debt (i.e., established as a liability) or available (i.e., not a debt but present with the contractor). For example, if a witness said, 'I have a thousand dirhams as a debt on Khalid,' then Khalid the debtor sold his specific property, say one hundred dinars, to the creditor in exchange for the debt. Then both agreed that the witness had no debt on Khalid. The said contract between the witness and Khalid would not be void. Rather, the witness becomes liable for the equivalent of the debt, i.e., one thousand dirhams. Observe: if dirhams and dinars became specified by specification, then when they agreed that there was no debt on Khalid, the contract would become void because the dirhams that were specified as a debt no longer exist. But the contract is not void. Thus it is proven that dirhams and dinars, whether debt or available, are not specified by specification in exchange contracts. And something that is not specified by specification, in it general and specific are equal. The general case is when the creditor says to the debtor, 'Buy a slave for me in exchange for one thousand dirhams,' without relating those thousand to the debt. The specific case is when he relates them to the debt, e.g., 'Buy a slave for me in exchange for those one thousand that you owe me.' Since dirhams and dinars are not specified by specification, and in that general and specific are equal, the creditor's statement to his debtor, 'Buy a slave for me in exchange for these one thousand dirhams that you owe me' and his statement 'Buy a slave for me in exchange for one thousand dirhams' are equal. And if the creditor says, 'Buy a slave for me in exchange for one thousand dirhams' without relating it to the debt, this agency is valid by consensus. Therefore, if he relates it to the debt, the agency is also valid. And when the agency is valid in this case, the purchase of the agent is binding on the principal. So if the slave dies in the possession of the agent before the principal takes possession, it dies from the principal's property, i.e., the death of the slave is a loss for the principal, not
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the validity of an agency where a creditor appoints his debtor as agent to purchase a specific or unspecified item using the debt as the price. The author first states the ruling of Abu Hanifa: if the slave dies before the principal takes possession, the loss is on the agent (debtor), but if the principal takes possession, the loss is on the principal. This is because Abu Hanifa considers that coins (dirhams and dinars) become specified in agency contracts, so the agency is invalid when the price is the debt. The Sahibayn argue that coins are not specified in exchange contracts, so the agency is valid and the agent's possession is like the principal's possession, making the purchase binding on the principal. The extension to salam and sarf contracts follows the same logic. The author then provides a detailed explanation (tashrih) of the case, clarifying that the dispute applies only when the creditor does not specify the seller or counterparty. If specified, the agency is valid by consensus. The Sahibayn's proof uses an analogy: if a witness sold property in exchange for a debt that later was agreed not to exist, the contract remains valid, showing that coins are not specified. Hence, general and specific expressions are equal. The lesson concludes that when specified, the agency is valid, and the loss from the slave's death before possession falls on the principal.

Key Terms
توکیل (tawkil): The act of appointing an agent (wakil) to act on one's behalf in a legal transaction.
مؤکل (muwakkil): The principal who appoints an agent. In this passage, the creditor is the muwakkil.
وکیل (wakil): The agent appointed to act on behalf of the principal. Here, the debtor is the wakil.
تعاطی (mu'atah): A contract concluded by mutual exchange without formal offer and acceptance, considered valid in Hanafi law for sale transactions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, if a creditor appoints his debtor as agent to buy a slave with the debt, and the slave dies in the debtor's possession before the creditor takes possession, who bears the loss?
A) The creditor (muwakkil) bears the loss and the debt is discharged.
B) The debtor (wakil) bears the loss and the debt remains due.
C) The seller bears the loss.
D) The loss is shared equally.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the validity of an agency to purchase with a debt depends on whether coins are considered specified in agency contracts—Abu Hanifa says yes (invalid), the Sahibayn say no (valid), and the latter is the mufta bihi in transactions.

Slave Agent Buying Himself: Self-Purchase Becomes Emancipation
Ruling on a slave agent buying himself from his master for himself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a slave's self-purchase is treated as emancipation and distinguish the outcome when the slave attributes the purchase to himself versus to the principal.

Core Idea
A slave cannot legally buy himself; the 'sale' is recharacterized as emancipation (i'taq) due to lack of ownership capacity.

This passage from the Hidayah examines a nuanced case: a slave appointed as an agent to buy himself for his principal. If the slave attributes the purchase to himself, the transaction becomes legally impossible because a slave cannot own property. Hanafi law therefore reinterprets the agreement as an act of emancipation by the master. Understanding this ruling requires grasp of the slave's legal incapacity and the principle of recharacterization when a contract fails on its primary purpose.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The slave agent can either attribute the purchase to his principal (valid sale) or to himself (emancipation). Recognize that the passage focuses on the second case.
The first scenario (attribution to principal) is mentioned as a contrast.
2
Understand the slave's legal incapacity
A slave is not a legal owner of property (malik). Therefore, he cannot offer consideration in a sale where he is both buyer and object. This makes a self-purchase impossible as a commutative contract.
This is the core rational dalil: 'Al-'abdu la yamliku.'
3
Apply the recharacterization principle
When a contract fails as a sale due to impossibility, Hanafi law seeks a valid construction. Here, the master's 'sale' to the slave's own hand is legally an emancipation (i'taq), and the slave's acceptance is acceptance of freedom.
The master is deemed consenting to emancipation because he knows the legal effect.
4
Determine the effect of deviating from agency
The slave was appointed to buy for the principal, but by contracting for himself, he exceeded his mandate. Therefore, the contract does not bind the principal; it takes effect between the master and the slave (as emancipation).
If he had followed the mandate, the sale would be for the principal.
5
State the final ruling
The slave becomes free immediately. No price is owed because the contract is not a sale but emancipation.
The ruling is mufta bihi and from Zahir al-Riwayah.

So when he attributes it to the principal... etc. The result of the proof is mentioned, meaning it has been previously established that a slave can be an agent to buy himself from someone other than himself. So when the slave (agent) attributed the contract to his principal and said to his master (seller): 'Sell me myself to my hand for such-and-such a price for my such-and-such principal.' And it has already passed that this action of the slave – buying himself – can also be compliance with the command of his principal. And in the case where the agent attributes the contract to his principal and the agent's action is also in compliance with the principal's command, then in that case the contract takes effect for the principal, not for the agent. Hence here too, the contract of purchase will take effect for the principal, not for the slave (agent). If the slave made the contract for himself, he becomes free. 'And if he made the contract for himself, he is free because it is emancipation, and the master is pleased with it, not with a commutative contract. And the slave, even if he was an agent for the purchase of a specific thing, but he brought a different type of transaction, and in such a case, the transaction takes effect on the agent.' Translation: And if the slave made the contract for himself, he is free because this is emancipation, and the master agreed to it, not to a commutative contract. And the slave, even if he was an agent for the purchase of a specific slave, but he brought a different type of transaction, and in such a case, the transaction takes effect on the agent. Explanation: In this phrase, the second of the three mentioned cases is stated. Its summary is: If a person appointed a slave as an agent to buy himself from his master for him, then the slave did not attribute the contract of purchase to his principal but attributed it to himself and said to his master: 'Sell me to my hand for such-and-such amount.' Then the master said: 'I have sold.' Then this slave becomes free, because a slave does not own anything, and when a slave does not own anything, the question of buying himself in exchange does not arise. When this is the case, this contract will not be a purchase for the slave, but rather the master's sale of the slave's self to his own hand is an emancipation ('itq) on the part of the master, and the slave's buying himself is an acceptance of emancipation. In any case, this contract is emancipation, and the master is also pleased with emancipation, because the master knows that selling his slave's self to his hand is emancipation, and the master is not pleased with a commutative contract. And when the master is not pleased with a commutative contract, this contract will not take effect for the principal either. In any case, when this contract is emancipation and not a commutative contract, the slave becomes free. 'And the slave, even if he was an agent for the purchase of a specific thing...' etc. is an answer to an objection. The objection is: In this issue, the slave is an agent for a specific thing, i.e., buying himself, and when a person is an agent for the purchase of a specific thing, he is not authorized to buy it for himself. Therefore, here too, the slave should not have been authorized to buy himself for himself; rather, this purchase should have been for the principal, and in that case, the slave's freedom is obvious.
Source pages p. 240

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a slave is appointed as an agent to buy himself from his master for the principal. Two scenarios are distinguished. First, if the slave attributes the purchase to the principal (e.g., he says to the master: 'Sell me for my principal'), then the contract is valid and binding on the principal because the slave is acting within his agency. This is treated as a normal sale where the slave mediates the purchase. Second, if the slave attributes the purchase to himself (e.g., he says: 'Sell me to myself'), the contract is not a valid purchase because a slave cannot own property and thus cannot buy himself. Instead, the master's act of 'selling' the slave to himself is legally interpreted as emancipation (i'taq). The slave's acceptance of this 'sale' is acceptance of freedom, and the master, knowing this, is considered consenting to emancipation. Since the slave's action here deviates from the specific mandate (buying for the principal) and falls into a different category (purchase for himself), it is not effective for the principal. The passage also answers an objection: if the slave was appointed to buy a specific thing (himself) for the principal, how can he buy it for himself? The response is that he was not authorized to buy for himself, so his act is outside his agency; hence it is not for the principal but becomes emancipation. The practical outcome: the slave becomes free.

Key Terms
اعتاق (i'taq): Emancipation of a slave; the act of freeing a slave by the master.
وکیل بشراء معین (wakil bi shira' mu'ayyan): An agent appointed to purchase a specific item (here, the slave himself).
عقد معاوضہ (aqd mu'awada): A commutative contract involving exchange of consideration (e.g., sale). Contrasted with emancipation, which is a unilateral act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the master always intends emancipation in such cases.
B) Because a slave cannot own property, so the 'sale' is treated as emancipation.
C) Because the agent exceeded his authority, so the contract is void.
D) Because the principal rejects the purchase.

Key Takeaway
A slave's self-purchase is legally impossible and is instead treated as emancipation, resulting in the slave's freedom.

Slave as Agent to Buy Himself – The Core Ruling
Explains ruling and proof for slave as agent to buy himself

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a free man appoints a slave to buy himself from the master, and explain the proof based on the slave’s dual capacities.

Core Idea
A slave can act as agent to buy himself because his humanity is separate from his property status.

This lesson unpacks a key scenario from the Book of Agency: a free man orders a slave to buy himself from the master. The ruling might seem paradoxical—how can property buy itself? Yet the Hanafi school validates it by distinguishing the slave's person (the agent) from his property value (the subject). Understanding this dual capacity is essential to grasp the logic of agency for one's own self.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case
State the exact scenario: a free man (orderer) tells a slave 'Buy yourself from your master for me.' The slave then says to the master 'Sell myself to so-and-so for such price.' The master sells, and the slave accepts.
The slave acts as agent (wakil) for the free man, not for himself.
2
State the ruling
The slave becomes the property of the orderer (the free man). That is the Hanafi ruling.
3
Identify the proof structure
The proof is a talil (rational argument) based on the slave's two capacities: (1) he is a human – capable of legal agency; (2) he is property – sale applies to him as property. These are distinct.
Because the two capacities are separate, the slave can act as agent for another to buy himself, just as he could buy other property of the master.
4
Evaluate the analogy
The text compares this to appointing someone (slave or free) to buy other master's property. If it is permissible there, it is permissible here.
The analogy strengthens the ruling.
5
Note the effect of the slave's possession
The slave's property value is in his own possession (since he is ma'dhun). Therefore, after sale, the master cannot detain the slave to collect the price.
This is a subsidiary point about the seller's right to detain.

Source Text

تشریح مذکورہ فصل جن دو مسئلوں پر مشتمل ہے ان میں سے دوسرا مسئلہ اس عبارت میں مذکور ہے۔ جس کی صورت یہ ہے کہ ایک آزاد آدمی نے ایک غلام سے کہا کہ تو اپنی ذات کو اپنے مولیٰ سے میرے واسطے اس قدر ثمن کے عوض خرید کر اب وکیل ( غلام ) عقد شراء کو اپنے مؤکل کی طرف منسوب کرے گا یا اپنی ذات کی طرف منسوب کرے گا یا اس کو مطلق رکھے گا یعنی نہ اس کو موکل کی طرف منسوب کرے گا اور نہ اپنی طرف منسوب کرے گا۔ پس اگر وکیل (غلام) نے عقد شراء کو اپنے مؤکل کی طرف منسوب کیا۔ یعنی وکیل (غلام) نے اپنے مولی سے یہ کہا کہ تو میری ذات کو میرے ہاتھ میرے فلاں موکل کے لئے اس قدر ثمن کے عوض کے فروخت کر دے۔ پس مولی نے فروخت کر دیا۔ اور غلام ، وکیل نے اس عقد کو قبول کر لیا تو یہ غلام مؤکل کیلئے ہوگا ۔ دلیل یہ ہے کہ غلام، اپنی ذات کو خریدنے کے سلسلہ میں دوسرے آدمی کی طرف سے وکیل ہوسکتا ہے۔ اسلئے کہ غلام کی ، حیثیتیں ہیں۔ ایک تو یہ کہ وہ آدمی ہے۔ دوم یہ کہ وہ مال ہے اور اس کی مالیت اس کے مولیٰ کیلئے ہوتی ہے۔ مگر آدمی ہونے کی حیثیت سے غلام پر مولی کا کوئی حق نہیں ہوتا ۔ پس ان دونوں حیثیتوں میں تغامر ہے۔ اور غلام کے آدمی ہونے کی حیثیت ، اس کے مال ہونے کی حیثیت سے بالکل اجنبی اور الگ ہے۔
یہی وجہ ہے کہ اگر غلام نے اپنی مالیت کا اپنے مولی کے علاوہ کسی دوسرے کیلئے اقرار کیا اور یہ کہا کہ میں فلاں کا غلام ہوں تو یہ اقرار درست نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ کسی شخص کو یہ حق نہیں ہے کہ وہ دوسرے کے مال کا کسی کیلئے اقرار کرے۔ اور بیع ، غلام پر اس کے مال ہونے کی حیثیت سے وارد ہوتی ہے نہ کہ اس کے آدمی ہونے کی حیثیت سے۔ یعنی غلام کی خرید و فروخت اسلئے ہوتی ہے کہ وہ مال ہے اور اسلئے نہیں ہوتی کہ وہ آدمی ہے۔ پس جب غلام کی آدمیت اور مالیت الگ الگ دو چیزیں ہیں تو غلام کو خود اس کی ذات خریدنے کا وکیل کرنا ایسا ہے۔ جیسا کہ مولیٰ کے اموال میں سے کسی دوسر نے مال کو خریدنے کا، کیل کرنا یا غلام کے علاوہ دوسرے آدمی کو غلام خریدنے کا وکیل کرنا ۔ اور غلام کو اس کی ذات کے علاوہ مولی کے دوسرے مال کو خریدنے کا وکیل کرنا اور غلام کے علاوہ دوسرے آدمی کو غلام خریدنے کا وکیل کرنا دونوں جائز ہیں۔ تو غلام کو خود اس کی ذات خریدنے کا وکیل کرنا بھی جائز ہوگا۔ بہر حال یہ بات ثابت ہو گئی کہ غلام دوسرے آدمی کی طرف سے اپنی ذات کو خریدنے کا وکیل ہوسکتا ہے۔
الا ان مالیته فی یدہ سے صاحب ہدایہ کہتے ہیں کہ غلام کی آدمیت اور مالیت میں اگر تغایر ہے اور یہ الگ الگ دو چیزیں ہیں۔ مگر چونکہ غلام مأذون لہ فی التجارت ہے۔ اسلئے اس کی مالیت خود اس کے قبضہ میں ہے۔ اور غلام کی مالیت خود غلام کے قبضہ میں ہونے کا اثر یہ ہو گا کہ مولیٰ اور غلام کے درمیان عقد بیع واقع ہونے کے بعد اگر مولی (بائع ) ثمن وصول کرنے کیلئے غلام کو روکنا چاہے تو اس کو یہ اختیار ہرگز حاصل نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ جب بائع ہیچ کو سپرد کر دیتا ہے تو پھر اس کوشن وصول کرنے کی وجہ سے مبیع روکنے کا اختیار نہیں ہوتا ہے۔ جیسا کہ مودع (جس کے پاس امانت رکھی گئی ہے) نے اگر مالک سے ودیعت کو خرید لیا اور ودیعت ، مودع کے پاس موجود ہے تو بائع مالک کوشن وصول کرنے کی خاطر ودیعت کا سامان روکنے کا حق نہیں ہوگا۔ کیونکہ ودیعت کا سامان مودع کے سپرد کر دیا گیا ہے۔
اور رہا متن کا مسئلہ یعنی جب غلام نے اپنی ذات کو اپنے واسطے خریدنے کا وکیل کیا تو اس صورت میں وکیل پر یہ بیان کرنا ضروری ہے کہ میں نے اس غلام کو خود اس کی ذات کے واسطے خریدا ہے۔ کیونکہ اس مسئلہ میں بائع یعنی مولیٰ کے حق میں دونوں عقد مختلف ہیں۔ اسلئے اس مسئلہ میں اگر وکیل نے شراء (خریداری) کو اپنے مؤکل ( غلام ) کی طرف منسوب کیا تو یہ مولی (بائع ) کے حق میں اعتاق علی مال ہوگا اور مولی ( بائع ) کیلئے ولاء ثابت ہوگی ۔ اور وکیل پر ثمن (مال) وغیرہ کا مطالبہ نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ پہلے گزر چکا ہے کہ اس صورت میں وکیل سفیر محض ہوتا ہے۔ حقوق عقد اس کی طرف راج نہیں ہوتے ہیں اور اگر وکیل نے عقد شراء کو اپنے مؤکل کی طرف منسوب نہ کیا تو یہ عقد بائع (مولی) کے حق میں مبیع ہو گا ۔ یعنی مولی غلام کو فروخت کرنے والا ہوگا اور وکیل اپنے واسطے خریدنے والا ہوگا۔ اور وکیل پر ثمن و غیرہ کا مطالبہ واجب ہوگا اور کبھی بولی اتاق علی مال کو پسند نہیں کرتا ہے۔ بلکہ اس معاوضہ محضہ کو پسند کرتا ہے جس میں مطالبہ ثمن وکیل سے ہوتا ہے۔ اور اعتاق علی مال کو نا پسند کرنے کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اعتاق کی وجہ سے اگر چہ مولیٰ کیلئے ولاء ثابت ہوتی ہے۔ مگر مولی عاقیلہ بھی ہے۔ اسلئے اگر اس آزاد کردہ غلام نے کوئی جنایت کی تو اسکا تاوان وغیرہ بھی مولیٰ پر واجب ہوتا ہے۔ پس جب مولی بسا اوقات اعتاق علی مال کو پسند نہیں کرتا اور معاوضہ کو پسند کرتا ہے تو وکیل پر واجب ہے کہ وہ اس صورت میں یہ وضاحت کر دے کہ میں نے اس غلام کو خود اس غلام کے واسطے خریدا ہے تا کہ مولی سوچ سمجھ کر جواب دے۔ چنانچہ اگر وکیل نے یہ وضاحت نہ کی تو یہ خریداری مؤکل یعنی غلام کے واسطے نہ ہوگی۔ بلکہ خود وکیل کے واسطے ہو گی ۔
فوائد علامہ ابن الہمام اور صاحب کفایہ اور علامہ بدرالدین عینی نے فرمایا کہ مصنف ہدایہ کا قول ولا مطالبہ علی الوکیل مبسوط کی کتاب الوکالت میں باب الوکالۃ بالعتق کی روایت ہے اور کتاب الوکالت کے باب وکالۃ الماذون والمکاتب کی روایت یہ ہے کہ ثمن کا مطالبہ وکیل پر ہوگا یعنی ثمن وکیل پر لازم ہوگا۔ مزید تفصیل اور دلائل فتح القدیر اور کفایہ میں مذکور ہیں ۔
آزاد نے غلام سے کہا تو اپنے مولیٰ سے اپنی ذات کو میرے واسطے خرید غلام نے اپنے مولیٰ سے کہا میری ذات کو فلاں کیلئے میرے ہاتھ اس قدر ثمن کے عوض فروخت کر مولی نے ایسے کر دیا تو غلام مؤکل کیلئے ہوگا
ومن قال لعبد اشترلی نفسک من مولاک فقال لمولاه یعنی نفسی لفلان بكذا ففعل فهو للامر لان العبد يصلح وكيلاً عن غيره فى شراء نفسه لانه اجنبي عن ماليته والبيع يرد عليه من حيث انه مال الا ان ماليته في يده حتى لا يملك البائع الحبس بعد البيع لاستيفاء الثمن فاذا اضافه الى الأمر صلح فعله أمتنا لا فيقع العقد للامر
Book of Agency. (The sale) occurs upon (the slave) from the perspective of his being property. Except that the slave's property value is in his possession, so much so that the seller does not have the right to detain him after the sale to collect the price. So when the slave attributes the contract to the (principal), his action becomes capable of carrying out the command. Therefore this contract will be valid for the principal. Explanation: The aforementioned section consists of two issues, the second of which is mentioned in this passage. Its form is that a free man said to a slave: 'Buy yourself from your master for me for such a price.' Now the agent (slave) will either attribute the contract of purchase to his principal, or to himself, or leave it absolute (neither attributing to principal nor himself). So if the agent (slave) attributed the contract of purchase to his principal, meaning the agent (slave) said to his master: 'Sell my very self to me for such and such principal for such a price.' Then the master sold, and the slave-agent accepted the contract, then this slave will belong to the principal. The proof is that a slave can be an agent on behalf of another person in buying himself. Because the slave has two capacities: one is that he is a human being, the second is that he is property, and his property value belongs to his master. But as a human being, the master has no right over the slave. So there is a distinction between these two capacities, and the slave's humanity is completely foreign and separate from his property status. This is why if a slave acknowledged his property value for someone other than his master, saying 'I am so-and-so's slave,' this acknowledgment is not valid. Because no person has the right to acknowledge another's property for someone. And sale occurs upon the slave from his perspective as property, not from his perspective as a human. That is, a slave is bought and sold because he is property, not because he is human. So when a slave's humanity and property value are two separate things, appointing a slave as an agent to buy his own self is like appointing someone to buy some of the master's other property, or appointing someone other than the slave to buy a slave. And appointing a slave to buy other property of the master (other than himself), and appointing someone other than the slave to buy a slave – both are permissible. So appointing a slave to buy his own self would also be permissible. In any case, it is established that a slave can be an agent on behalf of another person to buy himself. Except that his property value is in his hand – the author of al-Hidayah says that even though the slave's humanity and property value are distinct and separate, because the slave is authorized for trade, his property value is in his own possession. And the effect of the slave's property value being in his possession is that after the contract of sale occurs between the master and the slave, if the master (seller) wants to detain the slave to collect the price, he will have no authority to do so. Because when the seller gives over the sold item, he no longer has the right to detain it for collecting the price. Like if a depositary bought the deposited item from the owner, and the deposited item is with the depositary, the seller-owner does not have the right to detain the deposited goods for collecting the price, because the deposited goods have been handed over to the depositary. As for the issue of the matn: when the slave appointed (himself) as agent to buy himself, in that case it is necessary for the agent to explain: 'I bought this slave for his own self.' Because in this issue, from the perspective of the seller (master), the two contracts (emancipation for consideration and sale) are different. Therefore, in this issue, if the agent attributed the purchase to his principal (the slave), then from the master's perspective it would be emancipation for consideration, and the master would establish wala', and the agent would not be demanded for the price, etc. Because it has passed earlier that in this case the agent is merely an intermediary; the rights of the contract do not revert to him. And if the agent did not attribute the contract of purchase to his principal, then this contract for the seller (master) would be a sale – meaning the master would be the seller and the agent would be the buyer for himself. And the agent would be obligated for the demand of the price, etc. And sometimes the master does not like emancipation for consideration; rather he likes a pure exchange in which the price is demanded from the agent. The reason for disliking emancipation for consideration is that despite wala' being established for the master due to emancipation, the master is also the 'aqila (one who pays blood money) of his freed slave. So if this freed slave commits a crime, its compensation etc. becomes obligatory on the master. So when the master sometimes does not like emancipation for consideration and likes the exchange, it is obligatory on the agent that in this case he clarify: 'I bought this slave for the slave himself,' so that the master can think and answer accordingly. If the agent did not clarify, then this purchase will not be for the principal (the slave), but rather for the agent himself. Benefits: 'Allamah Ibn al-Humam, the author of al-Kifayah, and 'Allamah Badr al-Din al-'Ayni said: The author of al-Hidayah's statement 'and the agent will not be demanded' is a narration from al-Mabsut in the book of Agency, chapter of agency for emancipation. And in the book of Agency, chapter of agency of the authorized slave and the mukatab, the narration is that the price will be demanded from the agent, i.e., the price will be obligatory on the agent. Further details and proofs are mentioned in Fath al-Qadir and al-Kifayah. A free man said to a slave: 'Buy yourself from your master for me.' The slave said to his master: 'Sell myself to so-and-so for such a price, to me.' The master did so, then the slave belongs to the principal. And whoever said to a slave: 'Buy yourself from your master for me,' and he said to his master: 'i.e., myself for so-and-so for such,' and he did so, then it is for the orderer. Because the slave is fit to be an agent for another in buying himself, since he is a stranger to his property value, and the sale applies to him from the perspective that he is property, except that his property value is in his hand, so the seller does not own detention after sale to collect the price. So when he attributes it to the orderer, his action is suitable for executing the command, so the contract occurs for the orderer.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This is a commentary from Ashraf al-Hidayah on a ruling in al-Hidayah (the matn) regarding agency. The passage explains a specific scenario: a free man (the orderer) tells a slave to buy himself from the slave's master. The slave then negotiates with his master, saying 'Sell myself to so-and-so (the orderer) for such a price.' The master agrees and the slave accepts the contract. The question is: who does the slave belong to after this transaction? The ruling is that the slave belongs to the orderer (the free man), not to the slave himself. The proof is based on the distinction between the slave's two capacities: as a human being (who can act as an agent) and as property (which is the subject of sale). Since the slave is a legal person capable of agency, and his property status is separate from his person, he can validly act as an agent for another to buy himself. The commentary further notes that because the slave is authorized for trade (ma'dhun), his property value is in his possession, which affects the seller's right to detain him after sale for the price—the seller cannot detain him. A key condition is added: the slave-agent must explicitly attribute the purchase to the orderer (the free man) when making the contract. If he does not specify, and simply buys himself without attribution, the contract may be considered a direct sale between the master and the slave, making the slave the buyer for himself, and the agent (the slave) becomes liable for the price. However, if he attributes it to the orderer, it becomes an emancipation for consideration ('itq 'ala mal) from the master's perspective, with the legal consequence of wala' (clientage) for the master and no demand for price from the agent. The passage also notes a difference in transmissions within the Hanafi school: according to one narration (from al-Mabsut), the agent is not demanded for the price; according to another (from al-Kifayah), the price is demanded from the agent. This ikhtilaf is mentioned without tarjih here.

Key Terms
وکالت (wakalah): Agency: one person appoints another to perform a legal act on his behalf.
غلام (ghulam): A male slave who is owned by a master.
مولی (mawla): The master/owner of the slave.
اعتاق علی مال (i'taq 'ala mal): Emancipation for consideration: the master frees the slave in exchange for payment or service.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario described, when a free man tells a slave 'Buy yourself from your master for me,' and the slave attributes the purchase to the free man, who does the slave belong to?
A) The slave himself
B) The master (since the slave was property)
C) The free man (the orderer)
D) It is invalid because a slave cannot be an agent

Key Takeaway
A slave can validly act as agent for another to buy himself because his humanity is separate from his property status.

The Condition of Attribution – Why the Agent Must Specify the Principal
Explains the necessity of attribution to principal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the slave-agent must explicitly attribute the purchase to the orderer, and identify the consequences if he does not.

Core Idea
Without explicit attribution, the contract may be a direct sale, not emancipation for consideration.

The previous lesson established that a slave can be an agent to buy himself. But the text adds a crucial condition: the agent must explicitly state that he is buying for the orderer. This is not a mere formality – it changes the legal nature of the transaction from a sale to emancipation for consideration ('itq 'ala mal), which has different consequences for the master. Why would the master care? Because emancipation brings wala' and potential liability for crimes of the freed slave. This lesson dissects that condition and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two possible contracts from the master's perspective
When the agent buys the slave, the master sees either: (1) emancipation for consideration (if the agent says 'I buy him for himself' i.e., for the slave), or (2) a direct sale (if no attribution).
The key is whose behalf the purchase is made: for the slave (emancipation) or for the agent (sale).
2
Understand the master's preference
Some masters dislike emancipation for consideration because it imposes wala' and makes them 'aqila (liable for blood money). A pure sale avoids that.
The condition protects the master's right to know what contract he is entering.
3
State the ruling on attribution
The agent must clarify: 'I bought this slave for the slave himself' (i.e., for the principal). If he does not, the purchase is deemed for himself (the agent).
4
Apply the rule to the main scenario
In our case, the orderer is a free man, not the slave. The agent (slave) must say 'I buy myself for so-and-so (the free man)' to make the slave belong to the orderer. If he does not, the master may treat it as a sale to the slave himself.
The text says: 'If he attributed it to the principal, it is towards the master an emancipation for consideration... if not, it is a sale.'
